General approach: The triangle T is defined by a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1. The set of lattice points in T is all integer points in that region. We need to cover them with n lines. We can try to bound the total number of points covered by n lines if all are sunny. For any line L (not necessarily sunny), the number of points from T lying on L is at most n (since the line can intersect the triangle in a segment, and the maximum number of integer points on a line segment of length something). Actually we can bound more sharply: For a line with slope 0 (horizontal), it covers at most n points; for slope ∞ (vertical), at most n; for slope -1, at most n; for any other slope, the maximum is at most floor((n+1)/something) maybe smaller. But we need to find the maximum possible number of points from T that can lie on a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1. Let's denote L: y = mx + c (if not vertical). We need integer solutions (a,b) with a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1.

The points on L are of the form (a, ma + c). For integer a, ma + c must be integer. So if m is rational (p/q in lowest terms), then for a multiples of q, ma + c becomes integer, but also depending on c. But the set of a such that ma + c is integer is an arithmetic progression with difference q. So the number of such a in the range [1, n] (since a ≤ n+1 - b, but a can be up to n maybe) is about (n)/q. That's not large. If m is irrational, there is at most 1 integer point. So indeed sunny lines cannot achieve n points. However could they achieve n points if they are not straight lines? No. So we need to compute an upper bound.

But we also have the possibility of lines with slope 0 or ∞ or -1, which are not sunny. So if we want many sunny lines, we must still cover many points with them. But the total number of points N is about n^2/2. The capacity of n lines, each with at most something like maybe (n+1)/2 points (if sunny), is about n * (n+1)/2 = n(n+1)/2, which equals N. So it might be possible that sunny lines can achieve exactly N points if each sunny line covers about n/2 points. But we need to check if there is a construction with all sunny lines that covers all points. Let L be a line. We consider integer points (a,b) with a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1. The number of such points on L is at most the number of integer solutions to b = mx + c (or x = c if vertical). If L is not parallel to x-axis, we can write y = mx + c. For integer a, ma + c must be integer. The set of a for which ma + c is integer is an arithmetic progression with difference d (the denominator of m). Let's denote m = p/q in lowest terms, q > 0. Then ma + c is integer if and only if a is congruent to some residue modulo q, and also c is rational such that when multiplied by a yields integer? Actually if m is rational with denominator q, then ma is integer only when a is multiple of q (if we want ma to be integer). But if c is also rational with denominator dividing q, then we can get integer values. However for a line to contain integer points, the slope must be rational, and the intercept must be such that for integer a, y is integer. More precisely, if L contains two integer points, then slope is rational, and the line can be expressed as y = (p/q)x + r where p,q are coprime and r is rational; then for integer x, y is integer iff x ≡ something mod q and r is appropriately rational. But it's easier: The set of integer points on L (if any) is a 1-dimensional lattice with basis vector (q,p) (if slope p/q). So the difference between consecutive integer points on L (in terms of x) is q. So the number of integer points on L within a bounded region is at most the length of the intersection of L with the region divided by q, but also constraints from y≥1, a+b≤n+1. So we can get an upper bound.

First, we need to determine an upper bound on the number of points from T that can lie on a sunny line. Let's denote L is sunny if it is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So its slope m is not 0, ∞, -1. Let's find maximum number of integer points (a,b) in T with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1 that lie on L. We can attempt to prove: If L is sunny, then |L ∩ T| ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Actually for n=4, (n+1)/2 = 2.5, floor is 2. That's ≤ 2. So the bound is ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. But is it always ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋? For n=3, (n+1)/2 = 2, bound = 2. That's correct. But is it possible that a sunny line contains more than ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points if it is not rational? No, but rational with small denominator could yield many points. Let's derive a rigorous bound.

Consider a line L with slope m (finite). Write L: y = mx + c. For integer a, y is integer only if ma + c is integer. If m is irrational, then at most one integer a yields integer y (since if two different a's gave integer y, then m would be rational). So that's ≤ 1. So bound ≤ 1. So the interesting case is rational slopes. So we can assume m = p/q in lowest terms, with q > 0. Then the integer points on L are those where a ≡ a0 (mod q) for some residue a0, and also c is such that y is integer. More precisely, L contains integer points if and only if c is rational with denominator dividing q, and then the integer points are spaced by q in x. So the set of x-coordinates of integer points on L is an arithmetic progression with difference q. So the number of integer points with a ≤ A (some bound) is at most floor((A - a_min)/q) + 1, depending.

Now we need to bound |L ∩ T|. Since T is bounded, we can bound by considering the maximum possible number of lattice points on L within the triangle. The triangle's x-coordinates go from 1 to n (maybe up to n, because a ≤ n+1 - b, and b≥1, so a ≤ n). Actually a can be up to n (if b=1). So a ∈ [1, n]. Similarly b ∈ [1, n]. So the region is a right triangle of size n by n, but only points with a+b ≤ n+1. So the maximum a is n. So the x-range is 1..n. So we can bound the number of integer points on L by something like the maximum number of integer points in an arithmetic progression with difference q that lie within the interval [1,n] and also satisfy the y constraint. But the y constraint may further restrict the number. So we can attempt to prove:

For a sunny line L, |L ∩ T| ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Is that always true? Let's test with n=4, (n+1)/2=2.5 → bound 2. Good. With n=5, bound 3. With n=6, bound 3. But is there a sunny line with slope, say, 1/2 and that can contain 3 points for n=6? Let's see. For n=6, points with a+b ≤ 7. The points with a even (since slope 1/2) and y integer? For slope 1/2, line y = (1/2)x + c. If c is integer, then y integer when x is even. So x even: 2,4,6. Then y = 1 + c, 2 + c, 3 + c. Need to satisfy a+b ≤ 7. For x=2, b = 1 + c; a+b = 2 + (1 + c) = 3 + c ≤ 7 → c ≤ 4. For x=4, a+b = 4 + (2 + c) = 6 + c ≤ 7 → c ≤ 1. For x=6, a+b = 6 + (3 + c) = 9 + c ≤ 7 → impossible. So maximum points is at most 2, maybe 2 or 3 depending on c. So indeed bound holds.

Now we need to prove that for any line L not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0, the number of points from T lying on L is at most ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. That seems plausible, but we should be careful: Could a line with slope, say, 2 contain 3 points for n=6? For slope 2, y = 2x + c. Then a+b = x + (2x + c) = 3x + c ≤ 7. For x=1, 3 + c ≤ 7 → c ≤ 4. For x=2, 6 + c ≤ 7 → c ≤ 1. For x=3, 9 + c ≤ 7 → impossible. So maximum points is at most 2 (maybe 3 if c is such that for x=1,2,3 all allowed but 3 not allowed). So bound holds. So the maximum is achieved by lines with slope 1 (or slope 1/2 maybe). So we need to prove an upper bound.

But is it possible that a sunny line with slope -1? That's not sunny. So no issue.

Thus we have an upper bound: For a sunny line L, |L ∩ T| ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. However we must also consider vertical lines? They are not sunny, so not needed. So for all sunny lines, the maximum number of points from T they can cover is at most (n+1)/2, but since it's integer, ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. But we need a bound that is sufficient to prove impossibility when k is too large. Specifically, if we have n lines, with k sunny lines, then the total number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most (n - k) * (max points per non-sunny line) + k * (max points per sunny line). However points can be covered multiple times; but we only need that each point is covered at least once. So if the maximum possible number of distinct points that can be covered by n lines (with the restriction that exactly k are sunny) is less than N, then it's impossible. But the actual coverage might be less than the sum of capacities, but we can arrange lines to cover distinct points as much as possible, so the maximum possible distinct points covered by n lines with given k sunny is at most the sum of the maximum capacities of each line, because each line can cover at most that many points, and lines are distinct, but they could cover overlapping points, which would reduce distinct count. So to achieve coverage of all N points, we need that the sum of the maximum capacities (over sunny and non-sunny lines) is at least N, and also that we can arrange the lines so that the union covers all points. However it's not enough to have sum capacity ≥ N; we need a construction. For impossibility, we can show that any set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covers at most something < N points, i.e., the maximum possible distinct points covered is < N. Then such a set cannot cover all T. So we need to bound the maximum possible distinct points covered by n lines with exactly k sunny. That bound could be derived as: For each line, the number of points from T it contains is at most some bound. Since lines are distinct, the union's size ≤ sum of these bounds. But this sum bound might be > N, but we need a sharper bound that takes into account that sunny lines cannot achieve high bound, and non-sunny lines have high bound, but we also have the fact that some points may be covered by multiple lines, so the union size is at most the sum, but we want an upper bound that is too low to cover N. So if we can prove that for any set of n lines with exactly k sunny, the total number of points covered (distinct) is ≤ (n - k) * (something) + k * (something). But is it possible that the maximum union size is less than that sum? Yes, but we need an upper bound that is ≤ something. The sum is an upper bound, but if the sum is ≥ N, that doesn't prove impossibility. For impossibility we need to show that no matter how you choose the lines, the union cannot cover all N points. So we need a stronger bound that forces the union to be ≤ something < N. That bound could be derived from constraints like: Non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) can cover at most n points each, but they are limited in number; sunny lines can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points each. So the sum bound is (n - k) * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. That's an upper bound on the total number of points (distinct) that could be covered if all lines cover disjoint sets of points. But in reality, lines may overlap, so the union size could be less. But we want to show that for some k, even this sum bound is < N, then certainly impossible. So we need to compute S(k) = (n - k) * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Compare with N = n(n+1)/2.

If S(k) < N, then impossible. If S(k) ≥ N, it might still be impossible due to structural constraints, but we need to analyze.

But note: The bound (n - k) * n for non-sunny lines is not necessarily achievable because some non-sunny lines (horizontal or vertical) can cover up to n points, but slope -1 lines can cover up to n points as well. So indeed each non-sunny line can cover at most n points. So S(k) is an upper bound on the sum of capacities. However we need to consider that lines are distinct, but they could be of different types. For example, if we have both horizontal and vertical lines, they can cover many points. So S(k) is a crude bound. But maybe for some k, S(k) < N, then impossible. For k = n, S(n) = n * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Let's compute:

If n is odd, n = 2m+1, then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = ⌊(2m+2)/2⌋ = m+1. So S(n) = n * (m+1) = (2m+1)*(m+1). N = n(n+1)/2 = (2m+1)*(2m+2)/2 = (2m+1)*(m+1). That's exactly the same! So for odd n, S(n) = N. For n even, n = 2m, then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = ⌊(2m+1)/2⌋ = m. So S(n) = n * m = 2m * m = 2m^2. N = n(n+1)/2 = 2m*(2m+1)/2 = m*(2m+1) = 2m^2 + m. So S(n) = 2m^2 < 2m^2 + m = N. So for even n, the sum bound is strictly less than N. Therefore if all lines are sunny, it's impossible for even n. For odd n, it's not impossible by this bound, but we need to see whether it's actually possible. For n=3 (odd) it's possible. For n=5 (odd) we suspect possible. But need to check if there are any other constraints that might make it impossible for odd n > 3? Possibly we can construct.

Now for k = n-1, S(n-1) = (n - (n-1)) * n + (n-1) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = 1 * n + (n-1) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Compare with N = n(n+1)/2. Let's compute for odd n = 2m+1: then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = m+1. So S(n-1) = n + (n-1)*(m+1) = (2m+1) + (2m)*(m+1) = 2m+1 + 2m(m+1) = 2m+1 + 2m^2 + 2m = 2m^2 + 4m + 1. N = (2m+1)*(2m+2)/2 = (2m+1)*(m+1) = 2m^2 + 3m + 1. So S(n-1) > N. So the sum bound is not restrictive. So possibility depends on construction. So the bound only shows that for even n, k=n is impossible. For odd n, we need to see if k=n is possible. We have example for n=3, so likely possible for all odd n≥3. But we need to confirm for n=5,7,... Let's try to construct.

We also need to consider if there are any other restrictions: For k = n, all lines must be sunny. So we need to cover T with n sunny lines. That's a covering problem. We can attempt to use lines with slopes 1,2,3,... but we need to cover all points. Since the maximum points per sunny line is at most (n+1)/2 for odd n, and we have n lines, the sum bound is N, but we need to actually cover all points. - For i = 1,2,...,n, define line L_i: y = (i) x + c_i? But then we need to cover all points. Another idea: Use lines that are all parallel to the hypotenuse? Not allowed. But we could use lines with slopes that are the negatives of each other? Let's think systematically.

The points T are all integer points with a,b ≥ 1 and a+b ≤ n+1. This is essentially a triangular grid. A classic covering is by n lines: all horizontal lines y = 1,...,n. That's all non-sunny. If we want all sunny, we need to avoid horizontal, vertical, slope -1. So we need to find a covering with lines that have slopes not in {0,∞,-1}. This seems plausible if we use lines with slopes that are rational and such that each line passes through multiple points, and we have n lines, so average (n+1)/2 points per line. For odd n, (n+1)/2 is integer. So we can aim for each sunny line to cover exactly (n+1)/2 points. That's a perfect covering. For n=3, (n+1)/2 = 2, and we had lines covering 2 points each. For n=5, (n+1)/2 = 3. So we need 5 sunny lines covering 15 points, each covering 3 points. We need lines covering 3 points each. The only lines that can cover 3 points are those with slope 1 (y = x + c) if c is such that the points (t, t + c) with t from some range are within T. That suggests that for n=5, we cannot have all sunny lines covering all points, because the maximum capacity of sunny lines is limited. But the sum bound (n * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋) for n=5 is 5*3 = 15, which equals N. However that bound is based on the maximum per sunny line (3). But to achieve that bound, we need each sunny line to cover 3 points. However we only have one line (slope 1, intercept 0) that can cover 3 points; other lines cannot achieve 3 points. So the sum bound is not achievable. But is it possible that some sunny lines have slope -1? Not allowed. So indeed, to cover all 15 points with 5 sunny lines, we would need each line to cover exactly 3 points on average. But is it necessary that each line covers 3 points? Not exactly; the union could be covered by lines that cover overlapping points, but total distinct points is 15. If some lines cover fewer than 3 points, then others must cover more than 3 to compensate, but the maximum per line is 3. So the only way to have union size 15 is if each line covers exactly 3 points and those sets are disjoint. However it's also possible that some lines cover 3 points, others cover 2, but then the union might be less than 15 if the lines overlap? Actually if the union is 15, then the sum of capacities (maximum per line) is an upper bound on the union size, but not tight. But we need to find a set of 5 lines such that union size = 15. Since each line can have at most 3 points, the sum of the sizes of the intersections (counting multiplicities) is at most 5*3 = 15, but if some lines have fewer points, then the total sum of sizes (counting multiplicities) is ≤ 15. For union size to be 15, we need that the sum of sizes (counting multiplicities) is at least 15 (since union ≤ sum, but we need union = 15, then sum must be ≥ 15). But the sum of sizes (counting multiplicities) can be > union if overlaps occur. So to have union = 15, we need sum of sizes ≥ 15. Since max sum is 15, the only way to achieve sum ≥ 15 is if each line covers exactly 3 points (sum = 15) and there are no overlaps, i.e., the sets are disjoint. However it's possible that some lines cover 3 points and others cover 2, but then sum < 15, so union ≤ sum < 15, cannot be 15. So indeed for n=5, if all lines are sunny, to cover all 15 points, we need each sunny line to contain exactly 3 points from T, and these 3-point sets must be disjoint. That seems unlikely.

But is it possible that a sunny line contains more than 3 points? For n=5, we argued max is 3. Could a line with slope, say, 3/2 (slope 1.5) contain 3 points? Let's test: y = 1.5x + c, with integer a, need y integer. If slope 3/2, then a must be even to get integer y. For a=2, y = 3 + c; a=4, y = 6 + c. Then a+b ≤ 6. For a=2, b = 3 + c → a+b = 5 + c ≤ 6 → c ≤ 1. For a=4, b = 6 + c → a+b = 10 + c ≤ 6 impossible. So max points is 2. For slope 1/2, similarly. So indeed only slope 1 can give 3 points. So we have one line with 3 points. Then we need to cover 12 points with 4 lines. Each line can cover at most 2 points. So max union size is 3 + 4*2 = 11, which is < 15. Therefore impossible for n=5. But wait, we could use lines with slope 1 but intercepts that give 2 points (like y = x+1 gives (1,2),(2,3) only 2). Could we have a line with slope 2 that covers 3 points? For n=5, max a is 5. For slope 2, y = 2x + c. Points with a = 1,2,3,4,5. b = 2a + c. a+b = 3a + c ≤ 6. For a=1, 3 + c ≤ 6 → c ≤ 3. For a=2, 6 + c ≤ 6 → c ≤ 0. For a=3, 9 + c ≤ 6 impossible. So max points is 2 (a=1 and a=2 if c ≤ 0). So 2.

Thus indeed for n=5, impossible. But for n=3, we had lines with slopes that gave 2 points each, and the union covered all 6 points, with 3 lines. So the maximum sunny points for n=3 is 2, and we had 3 lines, sum capacity 6 = N, and we achieved it with three lines each covering 2 points, disjoint. That worked. So for odd n, is there a construction where each sunny line covers (n+1)/2 points? For n=3, (n+1)/2=2. For n=5, we need each line to cover 3 points, but that seems impossible. So maybe the pattern is: k=n is possible only if n is odd? But we saw n=3 works, n=5 doesn't. So it's not just parity. We need to determine all k for which there exist n lines (with exactly k sunny) covering T. The bound from sum of capacities gives necessary condition for impossibility: if (n - k) * n + k * M < N, then impossible, where M is the maximum points a sunny line can cover. But for k=n, M ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For n=5, M=3, but the sum bound is N, but the actual achievable maximum union size is less than N due to inability to achieve M for all lines. However we need to consider the possibility that lines can cover overlapping points, but that reduces union size. So to prove impossibility for k=n (when n is even or odd but not covering), we need to show that any set of n sunny lines covers at most something < N. That something is not simply n * M, because it's possible that some sunny lines cover M, others cover less, but we can try to maximize the union. The maximum union size is at most the sum of capacities, but it could be less. But we need an upper bound that is < N. If the sum bound is ≥ N, we cannot conclude impossibility. So we need to find a stronger bound that takes into account the structure of T and the fact that sunny lines cannot be horizontal/vertical/slope -1, which restricts the possible collinearities.

Thus we need to analyze the covering problem more carefully. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to characterize the possible k.

We have n ≥ 3 given. So we can try to construct examples for all k from 0 to n except perhaps some that are impossible. And prove that those impossible k cannot be realized.

Let's attempt to construct for each k. Idea: Use a set of n lines that are all horizontal (or all vertical) which gives k=0. Then we can modify by "replacing" some horizontal lines with sunny lines that still cover the same points (or more). But we need to ensure that the covering condition holds. If we replace a horizontal line with a sunny line that also passes through all the points that were on that horizontal line (or covers them), but we must keep lines distinct. However we can also add extra lines (but we have exactly n lines). So we need to choose n lines. The covering condition is that each point in T is on at least one line. So if we start with n horizontal lines (y=1,...,n), that's a valid covering. Now we want to increase the number of sunny lines. For k > 0, we need to have some sunny lines. We can try to keep some horizontal lines (non-sunny) and add sunny lines that cover points not covered by those horizontal lines. But we need exactly k sunny lines, so the other n-k lines are non-sunny. The covering must be satisfied. So we can try to design a covering where we use, say, (n - k) horizontal lines (or vertical) and then k sunny lines that cover the remaining points, making sure that all points are covered. The challenge is to cover all points with that combination. For large k, we need many sunny lines, but each sunny line covers few points, so we need to have enough non-sunny lines to cover many points. However if we have too many sunny lines, then the number of non-sunny lines is small, and the total capacity of the non-sunny lines might be insufficient to cover all points, but the sunny lines can also cover some points, but we saw for n=5, all sunny lines cannot cover all points. So maybe for n=5, k=5 is impossible, k=4 might be possible? Let's test. For n=5, we need 5 lines with exactly 4 sunny, 1 non-sunny. The non-sunny line could be horizontal (say y=1) covering 5 points. The remaining 10 points need to be covered by 4 sunny lines. Sunny lines can cover at most 3 points each (some may cover 2). So maximum union size from 4 sunny lines is 4*3 = 12, but they could overlap. The total distinct points from non-sunny line is 5, and we need to cover 15 points. The 4 sunny lines could cover at most 12 points, but they may also cover some points that are already covered by the horizontal line, so distinct total ≤ 5 + 12 = 17, but we need to cover 15 points. However we need to check if it's possible to cover all 15 points with 4 sunny lines and 1 horizontal line. The 4 sunny lines can cover at most 12 points, but we have 10 points to cover (since horizontal covers 5). However the 4 sunny lines might also cover some of those 5, but that doesn't help to cover the remaining 10. So we need the 4 sunny lines to cover all points that are not covered by the horizontal line. That's 10 points. So we need 4 sunny lines covering 10 points, each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, so maximum union from 4 sunny lines is at most 12, but we need exactly 10. That's feasible in terms of capacity. But can we actually find 4 sunny lines such that their union (with horizontal line) covers all 15 points? That seems plausible. For n=5, we could try horizontal line y=1 covering (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). Then the remaining points are:

(1,2),(2,2),(1,3),(2,3),(3,2),(1,4),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(1,5),(2,4) already? Let's list all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤6:

(1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1),(1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So after covering y=1 (i.e., b=1), points with b=1 are covered. So remaining points: (1,2),(2,2),(1,3),(2,3),(3,2),(1,4),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(1,5). That's 10 points. We need 4 sunny lines covering these 10 points. We can attempt to design lines that each cover 2 or 3 points. For instance, line L1: y = x (slope 1) covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (1,1) is already covered, (2,2) is in set, (3,3) is in set, so covers 2 points (2,2) and (3,3). That's 2. L2: y = 2x - 1 covers (1,1),(2,3) but (1,1) covered, (2,3) is in set. That's 1. L3: y = -2x + 5 covers (2,1),(1,3) but (2,1) covered, (1,3) is in set. That's 1. L4: y = (1/2)x + 1/2 covers (1,1),(3,2) but (1,1) covered, (3,2) is in set. That's 1. So not enough.

We need 4 lines covering 10 points. The maximum each line can cover is 3, so we need to achieve average 2.5. So some lines must cover 3 points. But which sunny lines can cover 3 points? For n=5, only slope 1 with intercept 0 covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) (3 points). That line also covers (1,1) which is already covered, but we can still count it as covering 2 of the remaining (2,2),(3,3). That's 2 points, not 3. If we take slope 1 with intercept -1, it covers (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) but (2,1) is covered, (3,2) is in set, (4,3) is in set? (4,3) sum 7 >6, not allowed. So only 2 points. Slope 1 with intercept 1 covers (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not allowed, so 2 points. So maximum points from a sunny line within T (without considering coverage of horizontal line) is 3 for slope 1 intercept 0, but that line includes (1,1) which is covered by horizontal line. So the set of remaining points it covers is at most 2. So perhaps we can design lines that are not slope 1 but still cover 3 points? For n=5, is there any line that contains 3 lattice points from T? Let's test systematically: The points are integer points in the triangle. A line containing 3 points must have slope rational with denominator q such that there are at least 3 integer points on the line within the triangle. The possible slopes are those where the difference vectors are multiples of (q,p). For q=1, slope integer, then points are spaced by 1 in x, so they are of form (a, ma + c). The number of such points with a ≤ n is at most n. But we also need a+b ≤ n+1. For integer slope m, a+b = (m+1)a + c ≤ n+1. For m≥2, (m+1) is at least 3, so a ≤ (n+1 - c)/3. For n=5, that gives at most 2 points (if c ≤ something). For m=1, a ≤ (n+1 - c)/2, which can be up to 3 if c=0. So slope 1 yields 3 points (with c=0). For slope -1, not sunny. So indeed only slope 1 (positive) can achieve 3 points. So the maximum number of points on a sunny line in T is at most 3, achieved by line y = x. So we have one line that can cover 3 points, but it also covers (1,1) which is covered by horizontal line. So when we have a non-sunny horizontal line covering b=1, the sunny line y=x can cover 2 of the remaining points. That's okay. So the total union from 1 horizontal line and 4 sunny lines might still be 15, but we need to design the sunny lines to cover the 10 points. Since we have 4 sunny lines, and each can cover at most 3 points, but many of those points are limited. Let's try to find a covering for n=5 with k=4. Perhaps we can use a different non-sunny line, not horizontal, but maybe slope -1 (non-sunny) to cover many points, and then sunny lines covering the rest. Let's explore possibilities.

But the problem asks to determine all k for which such a set exists. So we need to derive a condition on k based on n. The bound from sum of capacities (n - k) * n + k * M is necessary, but not sufficient. However we can also consider that non-sunny lines can be of three types, each covering at most n points. The sum bound is S(k) = (n - k) * n + k * M. If S(k) < N, then impossible. If S(k) ≥ N, it might be possible. We already computed that for k = n, S(n) = n * M. For n even, M ≤ n/2 (since (n+1)/2 floor is n/2) → S(n) ≤ n*(n/2) = n^2/2. N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2. So S(n) < N for even n. So impossible for even n. For odd n, S(n) = n * ((n+1)/2) = n(n+1)/2 = N. So S(n) = N. But as we saw for n=5, S(5) = 15 = N, but it's impossible because the bound is not achievable; the maximum union size from 5 sunny lines is less than N. So we need a better bound for sunny lines that takes into account the structure, not just the maximum per line, but also the fact that lines cannot be all of type that achieve that bound, and there is a combinatorial limitation.

Thus we need to analyze the covering problem thoroughly.

Let's denote T = {(a,b) ∈ ℤ^2, a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. |T| = N. We have n lines L1,…,Ln. Among them, exactly k are sunny. The others are not sunny, meaning they are parallel to x-axis (horizontal), y-axis (vertical), or line x+y=0 (slope -1). So each non-sunny line has slope ∈ {0, ∞, -1}.

Goal: Determine all k (0 ≤ k ≤ n) for which there exists a set of n distinct lines with exactly k sunny such that T ⊆ ⋃ Li.

We can attempt to construct for all k from 0 up to something, and prove impossibility for larger k. But the example for n=5, k=4 is likely possible. Let's try to design a covering with 1 non-sunny line (say vertical or horizontal) and 4 sunny lines. But we need to be systematic.

Alternative approach: Use a covering with lines that are all of the form x = c (vertical) or y = c (horizontal) or x+y = c (slope -1). These are non-sunny. We can choose a mix. For example, for n=5, we could take the 5 vertical lines x=1,…,5. That's 5 lines, all non-sunny, covering all points (since each point (a,b) with a≤5 is on line x=a). That's k=0. To increase k, we can replace some vertical lines with sunny lines. However we must keep lines distinct and cover all points. The covering condition is that each point lies on at least one line. So if we replace a vertical line with a sunny line that also passes through all points that were on that vertical line (or at least those points), then coverage is preserved. But can we find a sunny line that contains all points (a,b) with a = c (vertical) for a given c? That would be a vertical line, which is not sunny. So cannot. So we need to adjust the covering.

Another idea: Use the covering by n lines of type x = i (vertical) for i=1..n. That's non-sunny. Now if we want some sunny lines, we could add extra lines (but we must have exactly n lines). So we need to select n lines, some of which are sunny. So we cannot simply start from all non-sunny and replace; we need to construct a set with the desired k. We can attempt to design a family of lines that includes both non-sunny and sunny lines. For example, we could take (n - k) lines that are all horizontal (or vertical) covering a large portion, and then add k sunny lines that cover the remaining points. But we need to ensure that the total number of lines is n. So we need to cover all points with that combination.

Let's try to construct for n=5, k=4. We need 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line could be, say, the line y = 1 (horizontal) covering points with b=1. That's 5 points. The remaining 10 points (with b ≥ 2) need to be covered by 4 sunny lines. As we saw, the maximum union size from 4 sunny lines is limited. But maybe we can choose a non-sunny line that is not horizontal, but something like x+y = 2 (slope -1) covering (1,1) only. That's not helpful. We need a line that covers many points. The three types each can cover up to n points. The line y = 1 covers 5 points. The line x = 1 covers 5 points. The line x+y = 2 covers 1 point. So best is horizontal or vertical, covering n points. So we can take one horizontal line, say y = 1, covering all points with b=1. That's n points. Then the remaining points (with b ≥ 2) are N - n = n(n+1)/2 - n = n(n-1)/2. For n=5, that's 10. So we need to cover those with k sunny lines. So we need to see whether we can cover the "upper" part of T with k sunny lines. So the problem reduces to: For a given n, determine the maximum number of points from T (with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1) that can be covered by k sunny lines (with the rest of the lines being non-sunny, but we are focusing on the sunny lines covering a subset). But we also have the freedom to choose which non-sunny lines we use; we could use more than one non-sunny line to cover more points, thus reducing the burden on sunny lines. So for a given k, we can allocate some lines to be non-sunny (maybe all of the non-sunny lines) to cover many points, and then the sunny lines cover the rest. So the question is: for which k does there exist a covering where exactly k lines are sunny, and the other n-k lines are non-sunny (any type), such that T is covered. This is a covering problem with constraints on line slopes.

We can try to construct for each possible k, but we need to prove impossibility for some k. The necessary condition from sum of capacities (n - k) * n + k * M is that it must be ≥ N. But as we saw, for k = n (all sunny), this necessary condition is S(n) = n * M, and for odd n, S(n) = N, but it's not sufficient. For even n, it's < N, so impossible. So k = n is impossible for even n, and possible for odd n? We need to test n=3 (odd) works. n=5 (odd) doesn't work according to our reasoning. So maybe it's not all odd n; maybe it's only n=3? That seems unlikely. Let's test n=7 (odd). N = 7*8/2 = 28. M (max sunny points) = floor((n+1)/2) = 4. So n * M = 28 = N. So sum bound is sufficient. But can we cover all points with 7 sunny lines? Probably we need to see if there exists a set of 7 sunny lines that cover all points. For n=7, the points are a,b≥1, a+b≤8. The maximum points on a sunny line is 4 (achieved by slope 1 with intercept 0 covering (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4)). That's 4 points. For other slopes, fewer. So we have one line with 4 points, but then we need 6 more sunny lines covering the remaining 24 points. Each can cover at most 4, but many will be less. The sum bound is 7*4 = 28, but we cannot achieve that because the maximum union from 7 sunny lines is less than 28 due to inability to cover all points with lines of slope 1 only. But is there a configuration where each sunny line covers 4 points? That would require that there are 7 lines each containing 4 points from T, and the sets are disjoint (since total points is 28). That seems unlikely. So we need to analyze the structure of T and collinearity.

Thus we need to find a condition on k. Perhaps the answer is: all k with 0 ≤ k ≤ n, except k = n if n is even, and also except k = n if n is odd? But we have n=3 works, so that's not. So maybe the answer depends on n mod something. Let's try to construct for n=5 with k=4, and for n=7 with k=6? But we need to determine all k.

But we need to determine all k for which such a set exists. The problem statement: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. Let n ≥ 3 be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we are to find, for each n≥3, the set of possible k. So we need to characterize in terms of n.

We suspect that k can be any integer from 0 to n, except maybe some restrictions based on parity? But we saw n=4, k=4 impossible. n=5, maybe k=5 impossible, k=4 maybe impossible, k=3 possible? Let's test n=5, k=4 we attempted with one non-sunny line and 4 sunny, but seemed difficult. Let's attempt to design a covering with 2 non-sunny lines (say y=1 and x=1) and 3 sunny lines. That's k=3. But we have n=3 works, so not. So maybe the answer is: all k with 0 ≤ k ≤ n, except those k for which (n - k) * n + k * (floor((n+1)/2)) < n(n+1)/2. Let's compute that inequality.

We have N = n(n+1)/2. For a given k, the maximum possible number of distinct points covered by n lines (with exactly k sunny) is at most the sum of the maximum capacities of each line, but this sum is an upper bound. However if this sum is < N, then impossible. If sum ≥ N, it might be possible. But we also need to consider that sunny lines cannot all achieve that bound, but maybe we can use lines with slope 1 to achieve (n+1)/2 (if n odd) and other sunny lines to achieve less. The total sum bound might be achievable if we allocate the sunny lines appropriately. For n odd, we have M = (n+1)/2. Then S(k) = (n - k) * n + k * (n+1)/2. Compute S(k) - N:

S(k) - N = (n - k) * n + k * (n+1)/2 - n(n+1)/2.

Simplify:

(n - k) * n = n^2 - nk.

N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2.

So S(k) - N = (n^2 - nk) + k(n+1)/2 - (n^2 + n)/2.

Combine terms with n^2: n^2 - (n^2)/2 = n^2/2. Then we have -nk + k(n+1)/2 - n/2.

So S(k) - N = n^2/2 - nk + k(n+1)/2 - n/2 = n^2/2 - n/2 - nk + k(n+1)/2 = (n^2 - n)/2 - nk + k(n+1)/2 = n(n-1)/2 - nk + k(n+1)/2.

Simplify: n(n-1)/2 = (n^2 - n)/2. Then S(k) - N = (n^2 - n)/2 - nk + k(n+1)/2 = (n^2 - n + k(n+1) - 2nk) / 2 = (n^2 - n + kn + k - 2nk) / 2 = (n^2 - n + k(n+1 - 2n)) / 2 = (n^2 - n + k( -n + 1)) / 2 = (n^2 - n - k(n - 1)) / 2 = ( (n-1)n - k(n-1) ) / 2 = (n-1)(n - k) / 2.

So S(k) - N = (n-1)(n - k) / 2.

Check: For k=n, S-N = (n-1)(0)/2 = 0, consistent. For k=n-1, S-N = (n-1)(1)/2 = (n-1)/2. For n=5, that's 4/2=2 >0. So S(k) > N for k < n. So the sum bound is not restrictive. So we need a more precise bound for sunny lines that takes into account that they cannot all be of type that achieve M, but we can try to construct examples for all k < n. However we also need to consider that the non-sunny lines are not sunny, but they can be any of the three types. In our sum bound, we assumed each non-sunny line can cover up to n points. That's true. So if we have n - k non-sunny lines, they can cover at most n(n - k) points. But in practice, we might need to use multiple non-sunny lines to cover many points, but they could be overlapping. However we can choose the non-sunny lines to be, for example, all horizontal lines (y = 1,…, n - k). That would cover many points. But then the sunny lines cover the rest. The question is whether we can cover all points with that combination.

We should attempt to construct for any k (0 ≤ k ≤ n) a set of n lines with exactly k sunny covering T. Let's try to see if we can do it.

Approach: Use n lines, of which (n - k) are vertical lines (or horizontal) and k are sunny lines. But we need to cover all points. Suppose we take (n - k) vertical lines: x = 1,2,…, n - k. That's n - k lines. These cover all points with a ≤ n - k. The remaining points are those with a ≥ n - k + 1. The triangle T is bounded by a ≤ n. So the uncovered points are those with a from n - k + 1 to n, and b from 1 to n+1 - a. We need to cover those with k sunny lines. The number of uncovered points is sum_{a = n - k + 1}^{n} (n+1 - a) = sum_{t = 1}^{k} t = k(k+1)/2. That's the number of points in the "upper-right" part. So we have to cover those with k sunny lines. The maximum capacity of a sunny line in T is at most (n+1)/2 (for odd n) or n/2 (for even n). But note that the uncovered points are a subset; we can design sunny lines that pass through many of them. However we need to cover k(k+1)/2 points with k lines. That's an average of (k+1)/2 points per line. So we need each sunny line to cover about (k+1)/2 points. Is that possible? For k small, like k=1, we need to cover 1 point with 1 sunny line, easy. For k=2, we need to cover 3 points with 2 sunny lines. That seems plausible. For k=3, need to cover 6 points with 3 sunny lines. That's like n=3 case (all sunny). That's possible. For k=4, need to cover 10 points with 4 sunny lines. That's like n=4 case (all sunny) but we have 4 sunny lines, and we need to cover 10 points. However we earlier argued that for n=5, with k=4, we need to cover 10 points with 4 sunny lines. That's the same as the number of points in the "upper-right" part if we use vertical lines x=1,…, n - k = 1. That gives uncovered points with a ≥ 2 (since n - k = 1). That's exactly the set we considered earlier (10 points). So that's the same scenario. We tried to cover those 10 points with 4 sunny lines. It seemed difficult. So maybe it's impossible for k=4 in n=5. But we haven't proven impossibility. Let's try to find a construction with different choice of non-sunny lines, not necessarily all vertical. Perhaps we can use a mix of horizontal and vertical lines to cover more points, reducing the burden on sunny lines. For k=4, we have 1 non-sunny line. That non-sunny line could be horizontal (covers 5 points). Then the uncovered points are those with b ≥ 2 (since the line covers b=1). That's 10 points. That's the same as before. So that's one possibility. Could we use a non-sunny line of slope -1 (x+y = constant) that covers many points? The line x+y = 6 covers 5 points. That's also 5 points. So the set of points covered by a single non-sunny line is at most n, and we have n - k = 1 non-sunny line, so it covers at most n points. The uncovered points are N - (at most n) but we can arrange the non-sunny line to cover as many as possible, but we cannot cover more than n points with one line. So uncovered points count is at least N - n = n(n-1)/2. For n=5, that's 10. So we need to cover at least 10 points with k=4 sunny lines. So it's exactly 10 points, as we computed. So that's the worst-case (minimum uncovered) if we choose a non-sunny line that covers the maximum possible points (n). That's the best we can do to reduce burden on sunny lines. So if we cannot cover those 10 points with 4 sunny lines, then k=4 is impossible. But we also have the freedom to use more than one non-sunny line if k < n. For k=4, we have n - k = 1 non-sunny line, so we cannot have more. So indeed we must use exactly 1 non-sunny line. So we need to determine if there exists a set of 4 sunny lines covering all points not covered by that one non-sunny line. We attempted with horizontal line y=1, covering b=1. The uncovered points are those with b≥2. That's 10 points. We need to cover them with 4 sunny lines. Let's attempt to design such a covering systematically.

The uncovered points for n=5 with b≥2:

List T: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1),(1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). With b≥2, we exclude (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). So points:

(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5)
(2,2),(2,3),(2,4)
(3,2),(3,3)
(4,2)

That's 4+3+2+1 = 10 points. That's similar to before but not exactly the same as the set we considered with a≥2? Let's check: The set with a≥2 from T (excluding b=1) gave (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1) which includes some with b=1. So it's different. So we need to cover the upper part. That's 10 points.

Now can we cover these 10 points with 4 sunny lines? Let's try to find lines that contain many of these points. Some points have a=1: (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5). They are all on the vertical line x=1. That line is not sunny. So we cannot use that. But we can use lines that pass through some of those points with other points. For example, line through (1,2) and (2,3) has slope 1, sunny. That covers (1,2) and (2,3). Also (3,4) is not in set (since (3,4) sum 7 >6). So 2 points. Line through (1,3) and (2,4) slope 1, covers (1,3),(2,4). (1,4) and (2,5) not allowed. So 2. Line through (1,4) and (2,5) not allowed. So maybe lines with slope 1 can cover some pairs. Slope 1 lines (y = x + c) with c = 1,2,3,... For c=1, line passes through (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not in set. So covers (1,2),(2,3). For c=2, passes through (1,3),(2,4),(3,5) not allowed, covers (1,3),(2,4). For c=3, passes through (1,4),(2,5) not allowed, covers (1,4) only. For c=4, passes through (1,5),(2,6) not allowed, covers (1,5) only. So slope 1 lines give at most 2 points each.

Now consider lines with slope 2: (1,2) and (2,4) slope 2? (2-2)/(2-1)=0? Actually slope from (1,2) to (2,4) is (4-2)/(2-1)=2, yes. So line y = 2x covers (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) not allowed, covers (1,2),(2,4). That's 2 points. Also (1,2) and (2,4) are in set. Similarly line through (1,3) and (2,5) not allowed. So not.

Slope 1/2: (1,2) to (3,3) slope 1/2, but (3,3) is in set. So line y = (1/2)x + 3/2? Let's compute: line through (1,2) and (3,3) slope = (3-2)/(3-1)=1/2. Equation: y = (1/2)x + 3/2. That gives (2,2.5) not integer, (4,3.5) not integer. But the integer points on that line are (1,2) and (3,3) only. So 2 points.

Slope -1: not sunny. So many lines cover at most 2 points.

Now we need to cover 10 points with 4 lines. The maximum union size if each line covers at most 2 points is 8, insufficient. So we need some lines that cover 3 points. Are there any sunny lines that contain 3 points from this upper set? Let's examine the possibility of a line with slope 1 that passes through (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not in set. So no. Slope 2 maybe (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) not allowed. Slope 1/2 maybe (1,2),(3,3) and (5,4) not allowed. So only 2 points. Slope 3/2 maybe (2,2),(4,4) but (4,4) not in set. So none.

Thus it seems impossible to cover those 10 points with 4 sunny lines. Therefore k=4 is impossible for n=5. But is there a way to choose the non-sunny line to be something else, like vertical x=1 covering points (1,b) for b=1..5. That covers 5 points. Then uncovered points are those with a≥2. Thus for n=5, k=4 is impossible. What about k=5? That's all sunny. We already saw that seems impossible because sum bound is N, but the actual covering capacity is limited. We need to prove that for n=5, any 5 sunny lines cannot cover all points. But we already argued that with all sunny lines, the maximum number of points that can be covered is less than N. But is it possible that 5 sunny lines cover all 15 points if some lines cover 3 points and others cover 2? But we need total distinct points 15. The maximum union size from 5 lines with at most 3 points each is 15, but to achieve that, each line must have exactly 3 points and the sets must be disjoint. However we can have overlaps, but then union size would be less than 15. So to have union 15, we need all 5 lines to cover 3 points each and those 3-point sets must be disjoint. That seems impossible because we only have one line (y=x) that can cover 3 points, and that line's 3 points include (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). But note that (1,1) is also covered by many other lines (like horizontal y=1). But if we have all sunny lines, we cannot have horizontal y=1 (non-sunny). So we need 5 sunny lines covering all points. Let's try to construct explicitly for n=5 with 5 sunny lines. Maybe there is a covering using lines like y = x, y = x + 1, y = 2x - 1, y = (1/2)x, etc., but as we saw, max points per line is 3, but we have 5 lines, sum capacity 15. However to achieve 15 distinct points, we need 5 lines each with 3 points, and the sets must be disjoint. That seems unlikely. Could we have some lines with 4 points? The maximum is 3. So no.

Thus for n=5, k=5 impossible. But is k=3 possible? Let's test quickly with a construction. For n=5, we need 5 lines, exactly 3 sunny. So 2 non-sunny lines. We can try to use two horizontal lines (y=1 and y=2) to cover many points, then 3 sunny lines cover the rest. But we must ensure distinctness. Let's attempt:

They are distinct lines, so they cover distinct sets. The union of these two lines covers points with b=1 or b=2. That's 5+4=9 points. The remaining points are those with b≥3 and a+b≤6. That's points: (1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(2,3),(3,2) but (3,2) has b=2 already covered, so not. T for n=5: (a,b) with a≥1,b≥1,a+b≤6.

Points with b=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1).

Now if we take non-sunny lines y=1 and y=2, they cover all points with b=1 or b=2. That's 5+4=9 points. The uncovered points are those with b≥3: (1,3),(2,3),(3,3),(1,4),(2,4),(1,5). That's 6 points. (1,3),(2,3),(3,3),(1,4),(2,4),(1,5). 

We can attempt:

L1: y = x + 2 (slope 1) passes through (1,3),(2,4),(3,5) but (3,5) not in set, so covers (1,3),(2,4). That's 2 points.

L2: y = -x + 4? Slope -1 not sunny.

L3: y = x? (1,1) not in set. So no.

Maybe we can use lines that are not of slope 1 but something like y = 2x - 1? (1,1) not in set. (2,3) is on y=2x-1? 2*2-1=3, yes. (3,5) not in set. So covers (2,3) only. That's 1.

We need to cover 6 points with 3 lines. That's average 2. So likely some lines cover 2 points, some 1. But we have to cover all. Let's try to find a systematic covering.

We have points: (1,3),(2,3),(3,3),(1,4),(2,4),(1,5). Observing that (1,3),(2,3),(3,3) are collinear on the horizontal line y=3 (slope 0). That's not sunny. So cannot use that. Let's see if we can partition those 6 points into 3 collinear pairs, each pair lying on a sunny line (i.e., the line connecting them must not be parallel to axes or x+y=0). The pairs we can try:

- Pair (1,3) and (2,4) on line slope 1, sunny.
- Pair (2,3) and (1,4) on line slope -1? That's not sunny. So cannot.
- Pair (2,3) and (3,3) is horizontal (slope 0), not sunny.

- Pair (1,4) and (2,3) slope -1.

- Pair (3,3) and (1,5) slope -1 not sunny.

- Pair (3,3) and (1,4) slope (4-3)/(1-3)=1/(-2) = -0.5, sunny. That covers (3,3) and (1,4). Good.

P1: (1,3) – (2,4) (slope 1)

Now what about k=4? We need 4 sunny lines, 1 non-sunny. As argued, the non-sunny line covers at most n points. The uncovered points are at least N - n = n(n-1)/2. For n=5, that's 10. With 4 sunny lines, maximum union size is at most 4 * (max sunny points) = 4 * 3 = 12, but we need to cover 10 points. The necessary condition is sum of capacities ≥ 10, which holds (12≥10). But it's not sufficient because the actual coverage may be limited by geometry. We need to determine whether it's possible to cover 10 points (with a≥2, b≥2 maybe) with 4 sunny lines. That seems to be a rigorous bound? Let's examine that set more carefully: For n=5, the points with b≥2 (i.e., excluding b=1) are 10 points: (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). So k=4 impossible. Similarly, k=5 impossible. So the set of possible k for n=5 is {0,1,2,3}? But we need to confirm k=2 is possible. For n=5, k=2 would be 2 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines. That seems plausible. But we also need to consider k=1,0. So likely k ≤ 3. But is k=3 always possible for any n≥3? We need to construct. For n=3, we had k=3. For n=4, we had k=3 (1 non-sunny, 3 sunny). That's k=3 works. For n=5, k=3 works. For n=6, we need to determine possible k. Let's test n=6: N = 6*7/2 = 21. But is it possible to cover the 15 uncovered points with 5 sunny lines? The uncovered points would be those not covered by the non-sunny line. If we take the non-sunny line to be, say, horizontal y=1 covering (1,1)-(6,1) (6 points). Then uncovered points are those with b ≥ 2, N - 6 = 15 points. T: (1,1)…(6,1),(1,2)…(5,2),(1,3)…(4,3),(1,4)…(3,4),(1,5)…(2,5),(1,6). But careful: a,b≥1, a+b≤7. The maximum points covered by 2 lines is at most 2*n = 12, but we must consider that lines can be of different types and may cover overlapping points. The best we can do is to take two lines that are each of type that cover n points (horizontal or vertical) and ensure they are not the same line. So 11. So indeed two lines can cover at most 11 points? Let's test with a vertical line (x=c) covering up to n points, and a horizontal line (y=d) covering up to n points, but they intersect at (c,d) if c ≤ n and d ≤ n, so union size = n + n - 1 = 2n - 1. For n=6, that's 11. For n=5, that's 9. So indeed two lines can cover at most 2n - 1 points. That's the maximum. So with 2 non-sunny lines, we cover at most 2n - 1 = 11 points. Then uncovered points = N - (at most 11) = 21 - 11 = 10. That's what we saw. So we cannot reduce uncovered to 9 with 2 non-sunny lines. So if we have k=4, we have n - k = 2 non-sunny lines. The uncovered points are at least N - (2n - 1) = N - 2n + 1. For n=6, that's 21 - 12 + 1 = 10. So uncovered points are at least 10. With 4 sunny lines, max union is 4 * 3 = 12. So it's possible in principle, but we need to cover 10 points with 4 sunny lines. However we need to check whether any covering of 10 points with 4 sunny lines is possible. The earlier analysis suggested that from the set of uncovered points (which could be those with b≥3, or those with a≥2, etc.), the maximum points per sunny line is 2, so 4 sunny lines can cover at most 8, insufficient. But is it possible that with a different choice of non-sunny lines, the uncovered points set is such that some sunny line can cover 3 points? For example, if we take the non-sunny lines to be two lines of slope -1 (x+y = d) that cover many points, then the uncovered points might be those with small sums, and then some sunny lines (like y=x) could cover 3 points. Let's analyze systematically for a given n. The problem is to determine all k for which there exists a set of n distinct lines covering T. We can try to characterize possible k by constructing examples and proving impossibility for others.

Let's compute N = n(n+1)/2. The covering condition is that we have n lines. Among them, k are sunny. The maximum number of points that can be covered by n lines is at most the sum of the maximum capacities of each line, but also we need to consider that non-sunny lines can cover many points. So we need to find the maximum k such that there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny covering T. This is a combinatorial optimization.

We can try to derive an upper bound on k based on the total number of points. Suppose we have n lines, of which k are sunny. However we can try to bound k by considering the following: In T, there are points with a+b = n+1 (the hypotenuse). For n≥3, the number of such points is n. However if we have many sunny lines, we need to cover those points as well. But we can also cover them with non-sunny lines (like slope -1). So the constraint is not directly on k.

We need a systematic approach.

Let's denote:

T = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2, a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. |T| = N.

We have n lines L1,…,Ln.

Define for each line L, S(L) = |L ∩ T|. The covering condition is ⋃ L_i = T.

We want to find all k such that there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines (i.e., L_i not parallel to axes or x+y=0) covering T.

Observations:

- If a line is not sunny, it must be parallel to one of the three lines: x-axis (horizontal), y-axis (vertical), or x+y=0 (slope -1). So each non-sunny line can be of three types. So to cover all N points, we need (n - k) * (max points per non-sunny line) + k * M_sun ≥ N (ignoring overlaps). But this is a necessary condition. Conversely, we need to construct for possible k.

So we have:

That would be a stronger statement. So we need to determine, for a given n, the maximum possible size of T that can be covered by n lines with exactly k sunny lines. This is a covering problem where we can choose the lines arbitrarily. To maximize coverage, we would choose the non-sunny lines to be of the best type (horizontal/vertical/slope -1) that cover many points, and also choose sunny lines to be of the best type (slope 1) that cover many points, but we cannot have both if they overlap, but we can try to make the sets disjoint. The maximum possible union size is at most the sum of the capacities, but we can also consider that some lines might cover points that are not covered by others, so the sum bound is an upper bound on the union, but we can achieve the sum bound only if all lines are disjoint in terms of the points they cover. Define:

- Let A be the set of points in T that are covered by non-sunny lines. Let's compute: (n - k) * n = 12, subtract 2*3/2=3 gives 9, but we got 11. That's not matching. Actually I'm mixing. The sum of (n+1 - d) over d=1..m is m*(n+1) - m(m+1)/2. For m = n - k, that's (n - k)*(n+1) - (n - k)(n - k + 1)/2. That's not (n - k)*n unless n is small. So the maximum points covered by m non-sunny lines may be less than m*n if we use horizontal lines because each horizontal line covers less than n points (except the first). But we could use vertical lines similarly. However we can also use lines of slope -1 (x+y = constant) which cover points with sum d, and the number is d-1. The maximum is when d = n+1, covering n points. So a line of slope -1 can cover up to n points. So the best we can do for a non-sunny line is to have it be of type that covers n points, such as a horizontal line y = 1 (covers n points). But y = 1 covers n points, but also covers points with b=1. Similarly, x = 1 covers n points. So we can take (n - k) lines that are each of the best type (horizontal) to maximize coverage. However they might overlap, but we can choose them to be distinct horizontal lines y = 1,…, n - k. That's (n - k) lines. Their union size is sum_{d=1}^{n - k} (n+1 - d) = (n - k)*(n+1) - (n - k)(n - k + 1)/2. That's less than (n - k)*n. But is there a way to cover more points with (n - k) non-sunny lines by using a mix? For example, use some horizontal and some vertical, but they intersect, so union size may be less than sum of capacities. The maximum union size from (n - k) lines (each can cover up to n points) is achieved by taking lines that are all of the type that covers n points and are disjoint in terms of the points they cover. However can we have two lines that are both of type that cover n points and are disjoint? For horizontal lines, y = d covers points with b = d. Two different horizontal lines are disjoint in points because they have different b. So they are disjoint. Similarly, vertical lines are disjoint. So if we take m horizontal lines, the union size is sum of (n+1 - d) for d = 1..m. That's (m*(n+1) - m(m+1)/2). That's less than m*n. But if we take m vertical lines, same. So the bound we used earlier (n - k) * n is an overestimate of the maximum possible union size from non-sunny lines; the actual maximum is smaller. That condition is not necessary because if the actual maximum union size from non-sunny lines is smaller, then the inequality we derived (N ≤ (n - k)n + k M_sun) could still be true, but it's not a valid necessary condition because we used an upper bound on the sum of capacities, not a lower bound. Actually we derived: For any covering, N ≤ ∑ |L_i ∩ T| ≤ (n - k) n + k M_sun. The second inequality is true because |L_i ∩ T| ≤ n for non-sunny lines, ≤ M_sun for sunny lines. But is it possible that with a different selection of non-sunny lines, the uncovered set is such that some sunny line can cover 3 points? The uncovered set is T minus the points covered by the non-sunny lines. We want to maximize k, so we want to cover as many points as possible with non-sunny lines, leaving a set that can be covered by sunny lines. The maximum number of points that can be covered by (n - k) non-sunny lines is something we can compute. For a given k, we can try to choose the non-sunny lines to cover many points. However we also have the freedom to choose which sunny lines to use. The necessary condition for existence is that there exists a covering with n lines. To prove impossibility for some k, we need to show that for any such configuration, the union size is ≤ something < N. That something could be derived by bounding the maximum number of points that can be covered by n lines with exactly k sunny lines. We can try to find an upper bound on |⋃ L_i|. Since the lines are distinct, we can consider the complement: points not covered. But perhaps a more clever approach is to use a counting argument based on slopes.

Observation: The triangle T has N points. For a line L, define its "weight" as the number of points from T on L. If L is not sunny, then its slope is one of three: 0, ∞, -1. If L is sunny, its slope is not those three. Now, consider all lines in the plane that are not parallel to any of these three directions. The condition that we have n lines, exactly k of them are sunny, means that among the n lines, k have slopes not in {0,∞,-1}. The other n-k have slopes in {0,∞,-1}. We need to cover T.

Perhaps we can prove that if k is too large, then there must be some point in T that is not covered by any sunny line (since sunny lines can cover at most something). But we can also cover with non-sunny lines. However we only have n-k non-sunny lines, so they can cover at most some number of points. So we need to ensure that the total coverage (union) of n-k non-sunny lines and k sunny lines is T. If we can find an upper bound on the union size given that we have n lines, that is less than N for some k, then impossible. This is a covering problem. So we need to maximize the union size under constraints. That maximum is what we can achieve with a construction. If we can construct for all k up to some bound, then those k are possible. To prove impossibility for larger k, we need to show that any covering with that many sunny lines cannot cover all points. That is a different bound.

Maybe we can derive a bound using the fact that sunny lines cannot contain two points from the same "row" (horizontal) or "column" (vertical) or "anti-diagonal" (x+y = constant) unless they are parallel to those directions? Actually sunny lines have slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1. So they cannot be horizontal or vertical. Also they cannot be parallel to x+y=0. So they cannot be of slope -1. That's all.

Now consider the set of points with a+b = n+1 (the hypotenuse). This set has n points. Each of these points lies on the line x+y = n+1, which is not sunny. So they cannot be covered by sunny lines unless some sunny line passes through them. But a sunny line can pass through at most one point from that line? Actually if a sunny line passes through two points with a+b = n+1, then those two points would be on a line of slope -1 (since any two points on that line have sum constant). But a sunny line cannot be parallel to x+y=0, but it could intersect that line at a single point. Could a sunny line contain two points from that hypotenuse? If it contains two points with a+b = n+1, then the line connecting them would have slope -1 (since the line x+y = constant is slope -1). But a sunny line cannot have slope -1. So any sunny line can contain at most one point from that hypotenuse. Similarly, for points with a+b = constant (like the anti-diagonals), any line with slope -1 would be parallel to them. So sunny lines can intersect each anti-diagonal in at most one point. That's fine.

Now, the points on the hypotenuse are n points. If we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k points from that hypotenuse (since each sunny line can cover at most 1). The remaining n - k points from the hypotenuse must be covered by non-sunny lines. Since we have n - k non-sunny lines, that's enough, but we need to see whether it's possible to cover those points with the non-sunny lines given that non-sunny lines can be of type that cover many points. But we also need to cover the other points. So that's not a limiting factor.

Maybe we can use a pigeonhole principle on the number of points on each anti-diagonal. The anti-diagonals (x+y = d) have varying numbers of points. For d from 2 to n+1, the number of points is d-1. So the total number of points is N. If we have k sunny lines, each sunny line can intersect each anti-diagonal in at most one point. But we can also consider the intersection of all lines with a given anti-diagonal. This seems messy.

Another idea: Use an averaging argument based on the fact that each line L can be associated with a "direction". If we have many sunny lines, then they must have slopes different from 0,∞,-1. Perhaps we can show that the sum over points of something is bounded, and if we have too many sunny lines, then the total number of points covered by sunny lines is too low relative to N, so we need many non-sunny lines to cover the rest. But that's just the sum of capacities, which is not restrictive. So we need a more precise bound.

Let's compute, for a given n, what is the maximum possible k? We have examples for n=3 (k=3), n=4 (k=3), n=5 (k=3), n=6 (k=3). So perhaps the maximum is 3 for n≥4. But is n=4 maximum 3, n=5 maximum 3, n=6 maximum 3. Let's test n=7. Could we construct with k=4? We attempted and seemed impossible due to bound. But maybe there is another construction with different non-sunny lines that yields a covering of the uncovered set with 4 sunny lines. Now, what about n=3? We have k_max = 3 (all sunny). So the set of achievable k depends on n. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to describe the possible k for a given n (≥3). Based on our observations, we can conjecture:

- If n = 3, then all k ∈ {0,1,2,3} are possible.
- If n ≥ 4, then all k ∈ {0,1,2,3} are possible (except maybe some n where k=3 is impossible? But we saw n=4,5,6,7 all allow k=3). And k ≥ 4 is impossible.

So after removing b = 1,…, n-3, the remaining are b = n-2, n-1, n (and possibly n+1? b cannot exceed n because a≥1 → b ≤ n). So the number of remaining points is (n+1 - (n-2)) + (n+1 - (n-1)) + (n+1 - n) = 3 + 2 + 1 = 6. So indeed, regardless of n (as long as n ≥ 4), the remaining triangle has 6 points. That's interesting! However, the problem says "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following". So we need to provide a description based on n. Proof approach: Show that for any set of n distinct lines covering T, if exactly k of them are sunny, then k ≤ 3, and k ≠ 2 for n≥4? Actually we need to determine all k that can be realized. So we need to prove that for n≥4, k cannot be 2. Also that k cannot be ≥4. So the possible k are 0,1,3. But is k=2 impossible for all n≥4? Let's try n=4 we already saw impossible. n=5 impossible. n=6 impossible. n=7 impossible. So it seems plausible. But we need a rigorous argument.

We can attempt to prove that if k ≥ 2, then the number of non-sunny lines is n - k. The union of these non-sunny lines can cover at most something. But to get a bound on k, we can consider the points on the hypotenuse (x+y = n+1). There are n such points. Each of these points lies on the line x+y = n+1, which is not sunny. So any sunny line can contain at most 1 of these points. Also, non-sunny lines can contain many of these points. So if we have many sunny lines, they cover at most k of those points. The remaining n - k points must be covered by non-sunny lines. Since we have n - k non-sunny lines, that's okay. But we need to see if there is a contradiction when k = 2, but not when k = 1 or 3. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines could be chosen to cover all points except some. But we need to prove impossibility, so we assume there exists a set of n lines (with exactly 2 sunny) covering T. Then we derive a contradiction using combinatorial or geometric constraints. Perhaps we can consider the set of points with a = 1 (the leftmost column). There are n points there. Those points lie on the vertical line x = 1, which is not sunny. So any sunny line can intersect that column in at most 1 point (since if it passes through two points with x=1, it would be vertical, not sunny). So the sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from column a = 1. The remaining n-2 points from that column must be covered by non-sunny lines. Since we have n-2 non-sunny lines, that's possible. That doesn't give contradiction.

Maybe we can use a double counting of "pairs of points that are collinear with a sunny line". Alternatively, we can attempt to prove that the sum over all sunny lines of the number of points they contain is ≤ something, and also that the union of the lines must cover all points. Then we can bound k. But we already have that the sum of capacities is not restrictive. So need a different bound.

Consider the set of points with b = 1 (bottom row). There are n points there. Those lie on the horizontal line y = 1, which is not sunny. So any sunny line can contain at most 1 point from that row. Similarly, for b = 2, etc. So each sunny line can contain at most 1 point from each horizontal row (since if it contained two points with same y, it would be horizontal). However it could contain multiple points from different rows, as long as the slopes are not 0. So that's fine.

Now, the triangle T has N points. The number of points on each anti-diagonal (x+y = d) varies. Perhaps we can use the fact that a sunny line, not parallel to x+y=0, can intersect each anti-diagonal in at most one point. That's obvious. So that doesn't bound.

Another idea: Consider the points that are "isolated" in the sense that they have no other points collinear with them on a sunny line. But we can use an averaging argument based on the slope of lines. Suppose we have n lines. Among them, exactly k are sunny. Then the other n-k are not sunny. Each non-sunny line has slope 0, ∞, or -1. Now, consider the total number of points in T that lie on these non-sunny lines. Since they are limited, we can bound the number of points that can be covered by sunny lines. But we need to prove impossibility for k=2. Let's try to compute the maximum possible number of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines (subject to them being distinct and sunny). Then compare with N minus the points that can be covered by non-sunny lines. But we don't know the non-sunny lines' selection. We can try to derive an upper bound on |T| based on the number of sunny lines and non-sunny lines, assuming that we have n lines covering T. This is a covering problem; we want to see if there exists a covering with exactly 2 sunny lines. So we can attempt to construct a covering with 2 sunny lines for n≥4. If we succeed, then k=2 is possible. But we suspect it's not. So we need to prove impossibility. Let's attempt a systematic proof.

We have T = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2, a≥1,b≥1,a+b≤n+1}. |T| = N.

Suppose we have n lines L1,…,Ln, with exactly k sunny. We can consider the set of points in T that are not covered by non-sunny lines. Those must be covered by sunny lines. However it's also possible that some points are covered by both, but we need coverage. So we can say that the union of sunny lines must contain T \ U_n, where U_n is the union of non-sunny lines ∩ T. So |T \ U_n| ≤ |∪ sunny lines ∩ T| ≤ k * M_sun, where M_sun = floor((n+1)/2). So we need N - |U_n| ≤ k * M_sun. Since |U_n| ≤ (n-k) * n, we get N - (n-k) * n ≤ k * M_sun. This is necessary but not sufficient. However we can also get a lower bound on |U_n| given the lines? Actually we know |U_n| ≤ (n-k) * n, but we can have |U_n| smaller, so N - |U_n| is larger. So the inequality N - |U_n| ≤ k * M_sun is not guaranteed; it's a condition that must hold if covering exists, but we cannot assume it's true. However we can derive an upper bound on N - |U_n| (the uncovered points) from the fact that we have n lines covering T, so any point not covered by non-sunny lines must be covered by sunny lines. That doesn't give an upper bound; it's the other way: we need that sunny lines can cover those points. So if we want to prove impossibility for k=2, we can argue that for any choice of n-2 non-sunny lines, the number of points they can cover is at most N - 3 (as we computed). Then the uncovered points (T \ U_n) have size at least 3. Now, can those uncovered points be covered by 2 sunny lines? The maximum number of points that 2 sunny lines can cover is at most 2 * M_sun, but also the geometry may restrict. So if we can show that any set of 3 points from T (like those that are the complement of an optimal family) cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines, then k=2 impossible. But we need to consider all possible families of n-2 non-sunny lines. The complement may be different. However if we can prove that for any selection of n-2 non-sunny lines, the size of U_n is at most N - 3, then the complement size is at least 3. But that doesn't guarantee that the complement cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines; it's just that there are at least 3 points that need to be covered. We need to show that it's impossible to cover those points with 2 sunny lines, regardless of which points they are. However we can't guarantee that any complement of size 3 is not coverable by 2 sunny lines, because some complements might be three collinear points on a sunny line. But as we saw, the complements we get from optimal families are not collinear on a sunny line. Could there be a non-optimal family (with union size < N-3) such that the complement is of size >3, but that's even harder. So the only chance to have k=2 is to have a complement of size at most 2, but we can't because the maximum union is N-3. So the complement size is at least 3. So we need to see whether there exists a family of n-2 non-sunny lines whose complement is a 3-point set that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. If such a family exists, then k=2 would be possible. So we need to check whether any such family exists for n≥4. We already examined three families achieving maximum union, but those are the only ones that achieve union N-3? Are there other families that achieve union N-3? Possibly yes, but they would have to cover exactly N-3 points. The maximum union size from m non-sunny lines is N-3, as we computed, and it's attained when we take m horizontal lines with the smallest d (b=1..m) or vertical lines with smallest a, or slope -1 lines with the largest d (or smallest).
