Anti-diagonals d=4..6 cover 3+4+5=12? Actually d=4 gives 3, d=5 gives 4, d=6 gives 5, total 12. Which points are covered? d=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That includes (2,2). d=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That includes (2,3) etc. d=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). That includes (3,3). So covered. So not.

Thus the triples we get from optimal families are not the collinear ones. Could we create a family with a mix like {y=1,y=2, x=1}? That's 3 lines, union size 18? For n=5, that would be rows 1,2 and column 1. Compute union: y=1 covers 5 points, y=2 covers 4 points, overlap at (1,1) and (2,1) maybe? Actually y=1 covers (1,1)-(5,1). y=2 covers (1,2)-(4,2). Overlap between y=1 and y=2 is empty (different y). Overlap between y=1 and x=1 is (1,1). Overlap between y=2 and x=1 is (1,2). So union size = 5+4+4 - 2 = 11? Wait x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,5) = 5 points. So total union = (5+4+5) - overlaps: (1,1) counted in both y=1 and x=1, (1,2) counted in both y=2 and x=1. That's 2 overlaps, so union = 5+4+5-2=12. That's also 12. The complement is T minus those 12 points. Which points are missing? Let's list points of T for n=5:

b=1: (1,1)-(5,1) → 5
b=2: (1,2)-(4,2) → 4
b=3: (1,3)-(3,3) → 3
b=4: (1,4)-(2,4) → 2
b=5: (1,5) → 1
Total 15.

Now family {y=1,y=2,x=1} covers: y=1 gives (1,1)-(5,1). y=2 gives (1,2)-(4,2). x=1 gives (1,1)-(1,5). The union is points: (1,1)-(5,1); (1,2)-(4,2); (1,3),(1,4),(1,5) (but note (1,3),(1,4),(1,5) are not covered by y=1 or y=2). So union includes (1,3),(1,4),(1,5). Also (1,1) and (1,2) are already covered. So the covered set is:

(1,1)-(5,1) (6 points)
(1,2)-(4,2) (4 points)
(1,3),(1,4),(1,5) (3 points) = 6+4+3=13? Wait that sums to 13, but we need to check overlaps: (1,1) is in y=1 and x=1, (1,2) is in y=2 and x=1. Those are counted twice, but we subtract. So total unique covered = 5 (y=1) + 4 (y=2) + 5 (x=1) - overlaps (1,1) and (1,2) = 5+4+5-2=12. So indeed 12 points. Which points are missing? Let's list all points of T and see which are not covered:

Covered:
b=1: all (1,1)-(5,1) (5 points)
b=2: (1,2)-(4,2) (4 points) (but (1,2) is covered, (2,2) covered, (3,2) covered, (4,2) covered)
b=3: (1,3) covered by x=1; (2,3) not covered (since y=3 not in set, x=2 not in set; (3,3) not covered). So (2,3),(3,3) are not covered.
b=4: (1,4) covered by x=1; (2,4) not covered.
b=5: (1,5) covered by x=1.

So missing points: (2,3),(3,3),(2,4). That's 3 points. That's complement. Are these collinear on a sunny line? (2,3)-(3,3) horizontal, (2,3)-(2,4) vertical, (3,3)-(2,4) slope -1. So not.

Thus it seems that for any n≥4, the complements of maximum families are not collinear on a sunny line. But we need a proof that k=2 impossible. Perhaps a more clever argument: Consider the three points with smallest a+b (the "hypotenuse" of the triangle? Actually the points with a+b = n+1 are the extreme points. But maybe we can show that any sunny line can contain at most 1 point from the set of points with a = 1 (or b = 1), and also that the number of points with a = 1 is n, and the number of points with b = 1 is n. If we have only 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 * (max points per line) points from those columns. But we also have non-sunny lines covering the rest. However we can try to bound the total number of points covered by the sunny lines by 2 * floor((n+1)/2). But we need a contradiction when k=2. Let's attempt a double counting of "pairs of points that are collinear with a sunny line". Another approach: Suppose we have exactly 2 sunny lines. Then consider the set of points in T that are not on those two lines. Those must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are lines parallel to axes or slope -1. There are n-2 such lines. So the union of those lines must contain T \ (union of sunny lines). The union size of non-sunny lines is at most something, but we can also derive a lower bound on the size of T \ (union of sunny lines) based on the fact that the sunny lines can cover at most something. Let's compute the maximum number of points that 2 sunny lines can cover. Since they are distinct and sunny, they cannot be horizontal or vertical or slope -1. But they can contain many points. However we can bound the number of points they cover by considering the maximum number of points on a line in T (which is n) but they are sunny, so they can't be horizontal/vertical, but they could still contain many points, up to floor((n+1)/2) (as we argued). So each sunny line covers at most M = floor((n+1)/2). So 2 sunny lines cover at most 2M points. That's an upper bound. The remaining points (at least N - 2M) must be covered by non-sunny lines. Since we have n-2 non-sunny lines, they can cover at most (n-2)n points. But N - 2M might be larger than (n-2)n? Let's compute N - 2M for n≥4.

We have N = n(n+1)/2. M = floor((n+1)/2). So 2M is either n+1 (if n is odd, M = (n+1)/2, then 2M = n+1) or n (if n is even, M = n/2, then 2M = n). Then N - 2M is either n(n+1)/2 - (n+1) = (n+1)(n/2 - 1) = (n+1)(n-2)/2, or n(n+1)/2 - n = n(n-1)/2. So:

- n odd: N - 2M = (n+1)(n-2)/2.
- n even: N - 2M = n(n-1)/2.

Now, the number of points that can be covered by non-sunny lines is at most (n-2)n. Is it possible that N - 2M ≤ (n-2)n? Let's compare. For n odd, (n+1)(n-2)/2 vs (n-2)n. Compute (n-2)n - (n+1)(n-2)/2 = (n-2)(n - (n+1)/2) = (n-2)( (2n - n -1)/2 ) = (n-2)(n-1)/2. That's positive. So (n-2)n is larger than N - 2M, so it's not restrictive. For n even, N - 2M = n(n-1)/2, and (n-2)n = n(n-2). Compare n(n-2) - n(n-1)/2 = n( (n-2) - (n-1)/2 ) = n( (2n-4 - n +1)/2 ) = n( (n-3)/2 ). That's positive for n>3. So also (n-2)n > N - 2M. So the bound on union from non-sunny lines is not limiting. So that doesn't prove impossibility.

But we also have the fact that the sunny lines are only 2, and they cover at most 2M points. The remaining points (at least N - 2M) must be covered by non-sunny lines. However the non-sunny lines may not be able to cover those points because they are limited in number and also in their coverage pattern. The inequality N - 2M ≤ (n-2)n is necessary but not sufficient. However we can try to prove that for any set of n lines with exactly 2 sunny, the union size is ≤ something < N. That something might be derived by considering that the sunny lines can cover at most 2M points, and the non-sunny lines can cover at most something like (n-2)n points, but they could overlap, so the total distinct points covered is at most (n-2)n + 2M. But this is an upper bound, not a lower bound. To show impossibility, we would need to show that (n-2)n + 2M < N. That's false. So not that.

Thus the approach of bounding the maximum possible union size is not helpful. Instead, we need to prove that no covering with exactly 2 sunny lines exists. That is a structural result. Let's attempt to prove it by contradiction: Assume there exists a set of n lines L1..Ln with exactly 2 sunny lines covering T. Let S be the set of sunny lines. Then the complement of the union of the non-sunny lines (call it U_n) is a subset of T that must be covered by S. So |T \ U_n| ≤ |∪ S ∩ T| ≤ 2M. But also |U_n| ≤ (n-2)n. That doesn't give contradiction. However perhaps we can bound |U_n| from below, i.e., show that the non-sunny lines must cover many points because otherwise the sunny lines cannot cover the remaining points due to geometric constraints. For instance, if we have only 2 sunny lines, then there are many points that are not on those lines. Those points must be on non-sunny lines. So |U_n| must be at least N - (max points covered by 2 sunny lines). That's a lower bound. But we already have that upper bound is large, so not a contradiction. Actually we need a lower bound on |U_n| because if the non-sunny lines are few, they can cover many points, but we want to derive a contradiction with the covering condition. For a covering to exist, we need that the union of the non-sunny lines covers all points that are not covered by sunny lines. So the size of U_n must be at least N - (max points covered by sunny lines) (since sunny lines cover at most something). But that's not a lower bound; it's an upper bound? Let's think: If sunny lines cover at most 2M points, then the points they cover are some subset of T. The remaining points (N - (points covered by sunny lines) ) must be covered by non-sunny lines. So |U_n| must be at least N - (points covered by sunny lines). Since points covered by sunny lines could be less than 2M, the required |U_n| could be larger. So we have a lower bound on |U_n| depending on how many points the sunny lines actually cover. But we don't know that. However we can use the fact that the sunny lines are two lines, so they can cover at most 2M points. That gives |U_n| ≥ N - 2M. So the non-sunny lines must cover at least N - 2M points. But also the maximum they can cover is (n-2)n. So we need N - 2M ≤ (n-2)n, which holds. So no contradiction.

But we also have that the non-sunny lines are lines of three types. Their coverage is not arbitrary; they must cover the points that are not on the sunny lines. The problem is to determine all k such that there exist lines with exactly k sunny covering T. So we need to find constructions for some k, and prove that for others it's impossible. Based on our exploration, it seems that for n≥4, k can be 0,1,3. But is k=2 impossible? We need to provide a proof. Let's attempt a rigorous proof.

Let T be as defined. Suppose we have n distinct lines covering T, with exactly k sunny lines. We want to show that if n ≥ 4, then k cannot be 2. So we need to prove that any covering of T by n lines must have either 0,1,3 sunny lines (or maybe 2 is possible with a different construction that we haven't considered). But we already have a construction for k=3 for all n≥4, so k=3 is possible. k=1 and k=0 are trivial. So the only missing is k=2. To prove impossibility, we can attempt to derive a contradiction by analyzing the points that are "far" from the origin or something.

Idea: Consider the points with a = 1. They are (1,1),(1,2),...,(1,n). There are n points. They lie on the vertical line x = 1. That line is not sunny. So any sunny line can contain at most 1 point from this column. Similarly, for points with b = 1, they are on the horizontal line y = 1. That line is not sunny. So any sunny line can contain at most 1 point from that row. So if we have only 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 points from the left column (a=1) and at most 2 points from the bottom row (b=1). But there are many points there. The remaining points must be covered by non-sunny lines. That seems plausible. But is there a way to use this to get a bound on the total number of points that can be covered by non-sunny lines? Not directly.

Maybe we can consider the set of points that are "isolated" in the sense that they are not collinear with any other point on a sunny line. But perhaps a more systematic approach is to prove that if k ≥ 2, then the number of non-sunny lines is at most n-2. But we need to show that k cannot be 2. Let's try to see if we can construct a covering with exactly 2 sunny lines for some n≥4. The attempt for n=4 failed. For n=5,6,7 also seemed to fail. Perhaps we can attempt to find a construction by hand. Let's try n=4 with k=2: we need 4 lines, 2 sunny, 2 non-sunny. The non-sunny lines could be, say, y=1 and y=2 (covering 4+3=7 points). Then we need 2 sunny lines to cover the remaining 3 points. We argued that any sunny line can contain at most 1 of those 3 points because those points are not collinear on a sunny line. But is it possible that the 2 sunny lines together cover those 3 points if they are not both covering multiple points? For example, one sunny line could cover (1,3) and (2,3) (horizontal) but that's not sunny. So not. Could a sunny line cover (1,3) and (1,4) (vertical) no. Could a sunny line cover (2,3) and (1,4) (slope -1) no. So indeed, no sunny line contains two of them. So each sunny line can cover at most 1 point. With 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 points, but we have 3 points. So impossible. Similarly, if we use other non-sunny lines (like x=1 and x=2, covering 4+3=7 points, complement 3 points: (3,1),(4,1),(3,2) maybe, but check: for n=4, x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,4) (4), x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,3) (3). Union size = 4+3-1 (overlap at (1,1) maybe? Actually (1,1) is on x=1, not on x=2. So overlap between x=1 and x=2 is none because they are different columns. So union size = 7. The complement points are those not on these lines: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1) (since (4,2) not in T). That's 3 points. Are they collinear on a sunny line? (3,1)-(3,2) vertical, (3,1)-(4,1) horizontal, (3,2)-(4,1) slope -1. So no. So impossible. For n=5, we can try to use 3 non-sunny lines that cover 12 points, leaving 3 points. But we need to see if those 3 points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The complements we got from optimal coverings are triples that are not collinear on a sunny line. But maybe we could choose a different set of 3 non-sunny lines (not optimal) that cover, say, 11 points, leaving 4 points, and then use 2 sunny lines to cover those 4 points. That might be possible if those 4 points are all on 2 sunny lines (each covering 2). But we need to cover all points, so the sunny lines must cover the complement. If the complement size is 4, then with 2 sunny lines we could cover at most 2*M_sun = 6 points, so size 4 is possible. So we need to check whether there exists a selection of 3 non-sunny lines such that the complement (size at least 3) is exactly 4 or more, and those points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. But also we must ensure that the non-sunny lines cover all points of T except that complement. The union of the non-sunny lines must be T minus complement. So if the complement size is 4, then we need to cover 11 points with 3 non-sunny lines. The maximum union size from 3 non-sunny lines is 12 (for n=5), so it's possible to have union size 11 (by picking lines that are not optimal). So we could have 3 non-sunny lines covering 11 points, leaving 4 points uncovered. Then we need to cover those 4 points with 2 sunny lines. The question is: can we find 3 non-sunny lines (lines of slope 0, ∞, -1) that cover all points except some 4-point subset, and then find 2 sunny lines covering that 4-point subset. That seems plausible, but we need to examine whether such a configuration exists.

But the problem asks to determine all k such that there exist n lines with exactly k sunny lines satisfying the covering condition. So we are allowed to choose the lines arbitrarily. So if we can find a construction for k=2, then k=2 is achievable. So we need to either construct such a set or prove it's impossible. Our attempts so far suggest it's impossible. But we need a rigorous proof.

Let's try to prove that for n≥4, any covering of T by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines is impossible. Perhaps we can use a parity or counting argument based on the number of points on each anti-diagonal. Another thought: Consider the points that are "extreme" in the sense that they have a+b = n+1. There are n such points. Each of these points lies on the line x+y = n+1, which is not sunny. Therefore, any sunny line can contain at most 1 point from this set. So the two sunny lines can cover at most 2 of those n points. The remaining n-2 points must be covered by non-sunny lines. Since we have n-2 non-sunny lines, that's plausible. So that doesn't forbid.

But maybe we can consider the points that are on the boundary of T: those with a=1, those with b=1, and those with a+b=n+1. They are all "boundary" points. If we have only 2 sunny lines, then there are many boundary points that are not on those lines. They must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are limited in number. However they can cover many points each. But perhaps there is a combinatorial constraint: The set of points on T can be partitioned into "rows" and "columns" and "anti-diagonals". If we have a family of non-sunny lines, they must be of those types. The union of a certain number of such lines can cover many points. But the complement (the points not covered) must be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines are just two. The maximum number of points that two sunny lines can cover is at most 2M. But also they can cover at most something like the sum of the sizes of intersections with T. However perhaps we can prove that any set of two lines (sunny) can cover at most something like (n+1)/2 + something, but we already did. The complement size is N - |U_n|. Since |U_n| ≤ (n-2)n, complement size ≥ N - (n-2)n. For n=4, that's 2; for n=5, that's 3; for n=6, that's 4; etc. So the complement size can be as low as 3 if we achieve maximum union. So to have k=2, we need to achieve that the complement size is ≤ 2M (which is ≥ 4 for n≥4). That's okay. But also we need that the complement's points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. So the challenge is to prove that no matter which points are left uncovered by the non-sunny lines (subject to being the complement of a union of m non-sunny lines), those points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. Since we are free to choose the non-sunny lines, we can try to design them to leave a complement that is easy to cover. So the question reduces to: does there exist a 3-point subset X of T such that T \ X can be covered by (n-2) non-sunny lines? If such X exists, then we can set the non-sunny lines to be an optimal covering (size N-3), and then use 2 sunny lines covering X. But is it possible that X is covered by 2 sunny lines? If X has size 3, we need to see if there exists a pair of sunny lines covering all three. That's a geometric condition. For n≥4, we can try to find a triple X that is collinear on a sunny line (so one sunny line covers all three). That would be a valid X. But we also need that T \ X is coverable by n-2 non-sunny lines. So we need to check if any triple of collinear points on a sunny line (like y=x) is the complement of an optimal covering. For n=5, the points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) are on y=x. The complement of an optimal covering (rows y=1..3) is {(1,4),(2,4),(1,5)}. That's not that triple. But maybe we can create a covering with non-sunny lines that avoids covering those three points, while covering the rest. For that, we need to find (n-2) non-sunny lines such that their union is T \ X. Since the maximum union size is N-3, we need to achieve that size. The families that achieve N-3 are those where the non-sunny lines are the "extremal" ones (like rows 1..(n-2)). But those families cover many points, but they also cover the triple X? Possibly they cover X. However we can try to modify the selection to avoid covering X. For example, if we want to avoid covering (1,1),(2,2),(3,3), we could use, say, rows y=2,3,4 (for n=5) covering 4+3+2=9 points, but then complement is larger, not 3. So not.

But maybe we can use a combination of non-sunny lines that still cover N-3 points, but with a different complement. We saw that with n=6, the family {y=1,y=2,y=3, x=1} covered 12 points, complement was {(2,4),(3,4),(2,5)}. That complement is not collinear on a sunny line. So it's not trivial. But is it possible that some complement is collinear on a sunny line? Let's search for n=6. The points on y=x are (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) (but (3,3) is in T, (4,4) not). So that's 3 points. Could there be a covering by 4 non-sunny lines that leaves those three as complement? That would require that the union of those 4 non-sunny lines covers all points except those three. That's 18 points. The maximum union is 18. So we need an optimal covering that avoids covering those three points. The optimal coverings we know are rows y=1..4, columns x=1..4, anti-diagonals d=4..7. They cover those three points. But maybe there is another optimal covering, like a mix that leaves a different triple. Let's explore.

For n=6, we have 21 points. The maximum union size is 18. So any union of 4 non-sunny lines that covers 18 points is an optimal covering. The complement is a 3-point set. Which 3-point sets can arise as complements of optimal coverings? We can attempt to characterize all families of 4 lines (H,V,D) that achieve union size 18. Since the total points is 21, and the maximum union is 18, the complement size is 3. So we need to find families of 4 lines (each line is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) such that their union covers 18 points. There are many possibilities. Let's systematically analyze.

Define:

- For each row b (1 ≤ b ≤ 6), the points are (1..(7-b), b). The size is 7-b.
- For each column a (1 ≤ a ≤ 6), the points are (a, 1..(7-a)). The size is 7-a.
- For each anti-diagonal d (2 ≤ d ≤ 7), the points are (a, d-a) with a≥1, d-a≥1, a≤6, d-a≤6. The size is d-1.

Now, we have 4 lines. The maximum union size is 18. To achieve that, the lines must cover almost all points. The complement will be 3 points. Those 3 points must be such that they are not covered by any of the lines. Since the lines are limited, we can think about which points are forced to be covered if we choose certain lines. But perhaps we can prove a stronger statement: In any covering of T by n lines, the number of sunny lines is at most 3, and if it is 2, then the configuration must have some property that contradicts the covering condition. However our earlier examples for n=4,5,6,7 showed that k=2 is impossible. So we need to prove it generally.

Let's try to derive a contradiction using an averaging argument based on the fact that each sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points, and the non-sunny lines can cover at most something like (n-2)n points, but we need to consider the total number of points covered by the union. The union must be T. If we have exactly 2 sunny lines, then the total number of points covered by the union is at most (n-2)n + 2M. Since N = n(n+1)/2, we have (n-2)n + 2M = n^2 - 2n + 2M. Compare with N. For n even, M = n/2 → 2M = n → (n-2)n + n = n^2 - n. N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2. For n≥4, n^2 - n is less than (n^2 + n)/2? Let's compute: (n^2 + n)/2 - (n^2 - n) = (n^2 + n - 2n^2 + 2n)/2 = (-n^2 + 3n)/2. For n=4, that's (-16+12)/2 = -4/2 = -2, so n^2 - n > N. Actually N=10, n^2 - n = 12, so upper bound is larger than N. For n=5, n^2 - n = 20, N=15, so > N. So (n-2)n + 2M is greater than N. So that's not a contradiction.

But maybe we can improve the bound on union of non-sunny lines based on the fact that they must be distinct and also that they cannot cover all points because there are too many points on the hypotenuse that are not on those lines? Not.

Let's think differently. The problem likely expects a solution that determines the possible k. I recall a similar problem about "sunny lines" (maybe from an olympiad). I think the answer is: k can be 0, 1, 3 for n≥4, and 0,1,2,3 for n=3. But I'm not 100% sure about k=2 for n≥4. Let's try to construct a covering with k=2 for n=6 by hand.

We need 6 lines: 2 sunny, 4 non-sunny. Let's try to see if we can cover T with 4 non-sunny lines that cover many points, and then cover the remaining points with 2 sunny lines. The maximum union from 4 non-sunny lines is 18. So the complement size is 3. So if we can achieve that complement is a triple that can be covered by 2 sunny lines, then k=2 works. So we need to find a triple X of points in T such that T \ X can be covered by 4 non-sunny lines. Since 4 non-sunny lines can cover at most 18 points, they must cover exactly 18 points if we want to cover T \ X. So X must be a 3-point subset that is the complement of an optimal covering. So we need to find an optimal covering (union size 18) whose complement X is such that X can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The complements we found from optimal families are:

- Rows y=1..4: X1 = {(1,5),(2,5),(1,6)}.
- Columns x=1..4: X2 = {(5,1),(6,1),(5,2)}.
- Anti-diagonals d=4..7: X3 = {(1,1),(1,2),(2,1)}.
- Mixed: {y=1,y=2,y=3,x=1}: X4 = {(2,4),(3,4),(2,5)}.

Are there other optimal families? Possibly like {y=1,y=2,x=1,x=2}? But that would be 4 lines, union size would be? Let's compute for n=6, lines y=1,y=2, x=1,x=2. Union size: y=1 covers 6 points, y=2 covers 5 points, x=1 covers 6 points, x=2 covers 5 points. Overlaps: (1,1) appears in y=1 and x=1; (1,2) in y=2 and x=1; (2,1) in y=1 and x=2; (2,2) in y=2 and x=2. So we need to count unique. Compute using inclusion-exclusion. The total would be 6+5+6+5 - overlaps (but careful because overlaps are counted multiple times). The size of union of four sets can be computed. Let's do it systematically:

Points covered by y=1: (1,1)-(6,1) → 6
y=2: (1,2)-(5,2) → 5
x=1: (1,1)-(1,6) → 6
x=2: (2,1)-(2,5) → 5

Now, we need the union. Which points are covered? All points with b=1 (a=1..6) are covered by y=1. All points with b=2 (a=1..5) are covered by y=2. All points with a=1 (b=1..6) are covered by x=1. All points with a=2 (b=1..5) are covered by x=2. So the union includes all points with a≤2 and b≤2 (overlap), and also points like (3,1) covered by y=1, (4,1) etc., and (1,3) covered by x=1, (2,3) covered by x=2. So the union size is: rows b=1,2 cover 6+5=11 points. columns a=1,2 cover additional points that are not in those rows. The points not in rows 1,2 are those with b≥3. Those points could be covered by columns a=1,2 if they have a≤2. But points with b≥3 and a≥3 are not covered. The easiest is to have complement size 3, but we saw that complement of two non-sunny lines can be 3 points, but those 3 points may not be coverable by 2 sunny lines. So perhaps for n=4, k=2 impossible. For n=5, we have 3 non-sunny lines. The maximum union from 3 non-sunny lines is 12, complement size 3. So if we have 2 sunny lines, we need to cover those 3 points. As we saw, the complements we got from optimal coverings are triples that cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. But could there be a covering with 3 non-sunny lines that cover 11 points, leaving 4 points, and then cover those 4 with 2 sunny lines? That's possible if those 4 points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we need to check whether such a triple of non-sunny lines exists that covers 11 points and leaves a complement that is coverable by 2 sunny lines. The maximum from 3 lines is 12, so 11 is less than max. So we can try to find a set of 3 non-sunny lines whose union is 11 points. Then complement size is 4. We already considered rows y=1,y=2,y=4 which gives complement 4 points: (1,3),(2,3),(3,3),(1,5). Could those be covered by 2 sunny lines? Let's try to see if there exist two sunny lines covering those four points. The points are (1,3),(2,3),(3,3) on y=3, and (1,5). As argued, (1,3),(2,3),(3,3) are collinear on a horizontal line (not sunny). So each sunny line can contain at most 1 of these three. So with two sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 of them. The point (1,5) could be paired with one of them on a sunny line. That would cover 2 points, leaving 2 of the horizontal points uncovered, which would require at least 2 more sunny lines. So impossible. What about other triples of non-sunny lines? For n=5, the columns x=1,2,4 give complement 4 points: (3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(5,1). Similarly, (3,1),(3,2),(3,3) are vertical, each sunny line can contain at most 1. So not coverable. The D lines covering 12 points, complement 3 points, but we need to consider that maybe with a non-optimal set of 3 lines, we could leave a complement of 4 points that are all collinear on a sunny line? For example, can we leave the triple (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) as complement, and then cover them with one sunny line (y=x). That would be 3 points covered by one sunny line. But we need exactly 2 sunny lines, so we could have one sunny line covering those three, and the other sunny line covering some points already covered (redundant). But we also need that the union of the 3 non-sunny lines is T minus those 3 points. That is 12 points. So we need a set of 3 non-sunny lines that covers 12 points, leaving exactly those three. The optimal coverings (rows y=1..3) cover 12 points, but they also cover those three points. So they don't leave them. Could we modify to leave those three? For instance, instead of rows y=1,2,3, we could use rows y=1,2,4 (covers 11 points) and then add a column? But we need exactly 3 lines, and we can choose any combination. The maximum union is 12. So to have union size 12, we must achieve the maximum, which is attained by families where the lines are "extremal" (like rows 1..3, columns 1..3, anti-diagonals 4..7). Those families cover many points. The complement triples are those we listed. Those triples are not collinear on a sunny line. Could there be a different family of 3 lines achieving union 12 with a different complement? Let's check all possibilities.

We need 3 lines (H,V,D) that cover 12 points. Since total points is 15, complement size 3. So any such family must have complement of size 3. The complement is the set of points not covered. So we need to find families of 3 lines whose union is 12. The maximum union is 12. So families achieving this are optimal. There may be many. Let's enumerate all possible triples of lines (distinct) of types H,V,D such that they cover 12 points. Because the total points is 15, covering 12 means they miss exactly 3 points. So we need to see if any such triple can be chosen such that the missed points are all on a line that is sunny. The missed points are those not covered. Since the lines are of types H,V,D, the points covered are those that lie on at least one of them. The complement is determined by the lines. We can attempt to characterize all optimal families. This seems like a combinatorial geometry problem. I suspect the answer is that k=2 is impossible. Let's try to prove it generally.

Consider the set of points in T with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n points. Any sunny line can contain at most 1 of these points. Therefore, the two sunny lines together can contain at most 2 points from that set. The remaining n-2 points must be covered by non-sunny lines. Since there are n-2 non-sunny lines, it's possible to cover them with one D line (which covers all n points). But we could also cover them with multiple lines. So that doesn't restrict.

Maybe we can consider the points with a=1. There are n points. Any sunny line can contain at most 1 of them. So two sunny lines cover at most 2. The remaining n-2 points must be covered by non-sunny lines. That's possible with one vertical line x=1. So that's okay.

Now, the key is that the non-sunny lines are lines of three types, and they cannot cover all points unless they are chosen appropriately. But we need to show that for any selection of n lines with exactly 2 sunny, the union cannot cover all T. Perhaps we can use a parity argument based on the fact that each non-sunny line covers an odd number of points? Not.

Let's think about the structure of T. The points of T can be partitioned into "layers" based on a+b = d. For each d, the number of points is d-1. The total number of points is sum_{d=2}^{n+1} (d-1) = n(n+1)/2.

Now, suppose we have a family of non-sunny lines (H,V,D). Each line of type H (y = b) covers points with b constant. Its contribution to covering points is that it covers all points with that b. Similarly, V covers all points with a constant. D covers all points with a+b constant.

The sunny lines are lines with slopes not 0,∞,-1. They can cover points with varying a,b. But they cannot cover more than floor((n+1)/2) points each. So the total number of points covered by sunny lines is at most 2*floor((n+1)/2). That's ≤ n+1. So the points covered by sunny lines are limited. The complement (points not covered by sunny lines) must be covered by non-sunny lines. So we need to show that any set of (n-2) non-sunny lines cannot cover the complement of size N - |S∩T| (which is at least N - 2M). That seems not obviously impossible. But perhaps we can improve the bound on the number of points that (n-2) non-sunny lines can cover, given that they must also cover the points that are not on the sunny lines. However there is no restriction on which points they cover; they can cover any points. The maximum they can cover is (n-2)n, but that's not a bound for all selections. But we need to show that for any selection of (n-2) lines (of the allowed types), the union size is at most something less than N - (something) when k=2, leading to contradiction. Actually if we assume existence of a covering with k=2, then we have a specific set of (n-2) non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The union of the non-sunny lines must contain all points of T that are not on the sunny lines. That doesn't give an upper bound on the union of the non-sunny lines; it gives a lower bound (they must cover at least N - |S∩T| points). The union of non-sunny lines could be larger, but it must be at least that many. So we have:

|U_n ∩ T| ≥ N - |S∩T|.

Now, the maximum possible |U_n ∩ T| given that we have (n-2) lines of those types is something. But we also have an upper bound: |U_n ∩ T| ≤ (n-2)n, but that's not restrictive. However we can also derive a more refined upper bound based on the fact that the non-sunny lines are of three types, and they cannot cover all points because there are many points that are "far" from the axes and the anti-diagonal. But perhaps we can argue that the complement of the sunny lines must be large enough that it cannot be covered by the available non-sunny lines due to the specific geometry. For example, if we have two sunny lines, then the set of points not on them is a union of some subsets. The non-sunny lines must cover those points. But maybe we can show that the set of points not on any sunny line has size at least something, and that any family of (n-2) non-sunny lines covers at most something like (n-2)*floor((n+1)/2) points? That doesn't make sense.

Let's try a different perspective. The problem might be solved by considering the slopes of the lines. For a line L, define its "direction" as its slope. If L is sunny, its slope is not 0,∞,-1. So among the n lines, at most n are sunny. The condition that exactly k are sunny means that n-k lines are not sunny. Those lines are parallel to one of the three lines. Now, consider the set of points in T that are covered by lines of slope 0 (horizontal). These are rows. Similarly, columns and anti-diagonals.

Maybe we can use an averaging argument: each point in T lies on at most 2 lines among the n lines? Not.

Another idea: Use the fact that the triangle T is a convex lattice polygon. The lines that are not sunny are lines with slopes 0, ∞, -1. Their intersections with T are segments. The union of these segments covers a large portion. If we have only 2 sunny lines, then there are many points left. But perhaps we can prove that the number of points on the triangle that are not covered by the union of the non-sunny lines is at least 3, and those points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because any sunny line can contain at most 1 point from that set, and also the set is such that it contains three points that are not collinear on a sunny line. That's not a proof that it's impossible for some other selection.

We need to solve a combinatorial geometry problem. Let me restate:

We have n ≥ 3 given integer. We need to determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:
- For all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.
- Exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0.

Interpretation: The line is not parallel to x-axis (i.e., not horizontal), not parallel to y-axis (not vertical), and not parallel to line x+y=0 (i.e., slope ≠ -1). So sunny lines have slopes not equal to 0, ∞, or -1. They can have any other slope (including perhaps slopes that are undefined? Also note that lines that are parallel to any of those are not sunny; they are "non-sunny". So we need to have n distinct lines, exactly k of them are sunny, i.e., have slopes not in {0, ∞, -1}. The other n-k lines are not sunny, meaning they are parallel to either x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. Possibly some lines could be parallel to more than one? But lines parallel to x-axis are horizontal, they are not parallel to y-axis (unless they coincide? That's impossible unless they are degenerate. So each line is either sunny or not, and if not, it falls into one of three types: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. So we have n lines, with k sunny, and n-k non-sunny, each of which is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1.

Now the condition: For all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines. So count = n+1-c. For c from 1 to n, count = n, n-1, ..., 1. For c = n+1, count = 0 (not positive). So horizontal lines cover varying numbers.

Similarly vertical lines x = c cover points (c,b) with b from 1 to n+1-c. So count = n+1-c.

Lines with slope -1 (parallel to x+y=0) have equation x+y = d. For d from 2 to n+1, they pass through points (a,b) with a+b = d, a,b ≥ 1. The number of such points is d-1. So lines of slope -1 cover d-1 points.

Now sunny lines have slopes not 0, ∞, -1. They could be any other slope. They can be placed to pass through many points as well. But if c is also rational with denominator dividing q, then we can get integer values. However for a line to contain integer points, the slope must be rational, and the intercept must be such that for integer a, y is integer. More precisely, if L contains two integer points, then slope is rational, and the line can be expressed as y = (p/q)x + r where p,q are coprime and r is rational; then for integer x, y is integer iff x ≡ something mod q and r is appropriately rational. But it's easier: The set of integer points on L (if any) is a 1-dimensional lattice with basis vector (q,p) (if slope p/q). So the difference between consecutive integer points on L (in terms of x) is q. So the number of integer points on L within a bounded region is at most the length of the intersection of L with the region divided by q, but also constraints from y≥1, a+b≤n+1. But is it possible that a sunny line contains more than ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points if it is not rational? No, but rational with small denominator could yield many points. Let's derive a rigorous bound.

Consider a line L with slope m (finite). Write L: y = mx + c. For integer a, y is integer only if ma + c is integer. If m is irrational, then at most one integer a yields integer y (since if two different a's gave integer y, then m would be rational). So that's ≤ 1. So bound ≤ 1. So the interesting case is rational slopes. So we can assume m = p/q in lowest terms, with q > 0. Then the integer points on L are those where a ≡ a0 (mod q) for some residue a0, and also c is such that y is integer. More precisely, L contains integer points if and only if c is rational with denominator dividing q, and then the integer points are spaced by q in x. So the set of x-coordinates of integer points on L is an arithmetic progression with difference q. So the number of integer points with a ≤ A (some bound) is at most floor((A - a_min)/q) + 1, depending.

Now we need to bound |L ∩ T|. Since T is bounded, we can bound by considering the maximum possible number of lattice points on L within the triangle. The triangle's x-coordinates go from 1 to n (maybe up to n, because a ≤ n+1 - b, and b≥1, so a ≤ n). Actually a can be up to n (if b=1). So a ∈ [1, n]. Similarly b ∈ [1, n]. So the region is a right triangle of size n by n, but only points with a+b ≤ n+1. So the maximum a is n. So the x-range is 1..n. So we can bound the number of integer points on L by something like the maximum number of integer points in an arithmetic progression with difference q that lie within the interval [1,n] and also satisfy the y constraint. But the y constraint may further restrict the number. So we can attempt to prove:

For a sunny line L, |L ∩ T| ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Is that always true? Let's test with n=4, (n+1)/2=2.5 → bound 2. Good. With n=5, bound 3. With n=6, bound 3. But is there a sunny line with slope, say, 1/2 and that can contain 3 points for n=6? Let's see. For n=6, points with a+b ≤ 7. The points with a even (since slope 1/2) and y integer? Now we need to prove that for any line L not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0, the number of points from T lying on L is at most ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. That seems plausible, but we should be careful: Could a line with slope, say, 2 contain 3 points for n=6? For slope 2, y = 2x + c. Then a+b = x + (2x + c) = 3x + c ≤ 7. For x=1, 3 + c ≤ 7 → c ≤ 4. But is it possible that a sunny line with slope -1? That's not sunny. So no issue.

Thus we have an upper bound: For a sunny line L, |L ∩ T| ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. However we must also consider vertical lines? They are not sunny, so not needed. So for all sunny lines, the maximum number of points from T they can cover is at most (n+1)/2, but since it's integer, ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. But we need a bound that is sufficient to prove impossibility when k is too large. Specifically, if we have n lines, with k sunny lines, then the total number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most (n - k) * (max points per non-sunny line) + k * (max points per sunny line). However points can be covered multiple times; but we only need that each point is covered at least once. So if the maximum possible number of distinct points that can be covered by n lines (with the restriction that exactly k are sunny) is less than N, then it's impossible. But the actual coverage might be less than the sum of capacities, but we can arrange lines to cover distinct points as much as possible, so the maximum possible distinct points covered by n lines with given k sunny is at most the sum of the maximum capacities of each line, because each line can cover at most that many points, and lines are distinct, but they could cover overlapping points, which would reduce distinct count. So to achieve coverage of all N points, we need that the sum of the maximum capacities (over sunny and non-sunny lines) is at least N, and also that we can arrange the lines so that the union covers all points. However it's not enough to have sum capacity ≥ N; we need a construction. For impossibility, we can show that any set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covers at most something < N points, i.e., the maximum possible distinct points covered is < N. Then such a set cannot cover all T. So we need to bound the maximum possible distinct points covered by n lines with exactly k sunny. That bound could be derived as: For each line, the number of points from T it contains is at most some bound. Since lines are distinct, the union's size ≤ sum of these bounds. But this sum bound might be > N, but we need a sharper bound that takes into account that sunny lines cannot achieve high bound, and non-sunny lines have high bound, but we also have the fact that some points may be covered by multiple lines, so the union size is at most the sum, but we want an upper bound that is too low to cover N. So if we can prove that for any set of n lines with exactly k sunny, the total number of points covered (distinct) is ≤ (n - k) * (something) + k * (something). But is it possible that the maximum union size is less than that sum? Yes, but we need an upper bound that is ≤ something. The sum is an upper bound, but if the sum is ≥ N, that doesn't prove impossibility. For impossibility we need to show that no matter how you choose the lines, the union cannot cover all N points. So we need a stronger bound that forces the union to be ≤ something < N. That bound could be derived from constraints like: Non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) can cover at most n points each, but they are limited in number; sunny lines can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points each. So the sum bound is (n - k) * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. That's an upper bound on the total number of points (distinct) that could be covered if all lines cover disjoint sets of points. But in reality, lines may overlap, so the union size could be less. But we want to show that for some k, even this sum bound is < N, then certainly impossible. So we need to compute S(k) = (n - k) * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Compare with N = n(n+1)/2.

If S(k) < N, then impossible. If S(k) ≥ N, it might still be impossible due to structural constraints, but we need to analyze.

But note: The bound (n - k) * n for non-sunny lines is not necessarily achievable because some non-sunny lines (horizontal or vertical) can cover up to n points, but slope -1 lines can cover up to n points as well. So indeed each non-sunny line can cover at most n points. So S(k) is an upper bound on the sum of capacities. However we need to consider that lines are distinct, but they could be of different types. For example, if we have both horizontal and vertical lines, they can cover many points. So S(k) is a crude bound. But maybe for some k, S(k) < N, then impossible. For k = n, S(n) = n * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Let's compute:

If n is odd, n = 2m+1, then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = ⌊(2m+2)/2⌋ = m+1. So S(n) = n * (m+1) = (2m+1)*(m+1). N = n(n+1)/2 = (2m+1)*(2m+2)/2 = (2m+1)*(m+1). That's exactly the same! So for odd n, S(n) = N. For n even, n = 2m, then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = ⌊(2m+1)/2⌋ = m. So S(n) = n * m = 2m * m = 2m^2. N = n(n+1)/2 = 2m*(2m+1)/2 = m*(2m+1) = 2m^2 + m. So S(n) = 2m^2 < 2m^2 + m = N. So for even n, the sum bound is strictly less than N. Therefore if all lines are sunny, it's impossible for even n. For odd n, it's not impossible by this bound, but we need to see whether it's actually possible. For n=3 (odd) it's possible. For n=5 (odd) we suspect possible. But need to check if there are any other constraints that might make it impossible for odd n > 3? Possibly we can construct.

Now for k = n-1, S(n-1) = (n - (n-1)) * n + (n-1) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = 1 * n + (n-1) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Compare with N = n(n+1)/2. Let's compute for odd n = 2m+1: then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = m+1. So S(n-1) = n + (n-1)*(m+1) = (2m+1) + (2m)*(m+1) = 2m+1 + 2m(m+1) = 2m+1 + 2m^2 + 2m = 2m^2 + 4m + 1. N = (2m+1)*(2m+2)/2 = (2m+1)*(m+1) = 2m^2 + 3m + 1. So S(n-1) > N. So the sum bound is not restrictive. So possibility depends on construction. So the bound only shows that for even n, k=n is impossible. For odd n, we need to see if k=n is possible. We have example for n=3, so likely possible for all odd n≥3. But we need to confirm for n=5,7,... Let's try to construct.

We also need to consider if there are any other restrictions: For k = n, all lines must be sunny. So we need to cover T with n sunny lines. That's a covering problem. We can attempt to use lines with slopes 1,2,3,... but we need to cover all points. Since the maximum points per sunny line is at most (n+1)/2 for odd n, and we have n lines, the sum bound is N, but we need to actually cover all points. It may be possible to cover all points with n sunny lines by using a family of lines where each line is a "diagonal" with slope something like 1, but also include other lines. For n=3, we used three lines with slopes 1, -2, -0.5 (all sunny). For n=5, we could try to use lines that are like y = x + c, y = 2x + c, etc., covering points. But we need to ensure that every point lies on at least one line. Since we have many lines, we can design them to cover all points. However we must also have exactly n lines. So we need to find a set of n lines (sunning) that covers T. For odd n, maybe we can take lines with equations:

- For i = 1,2,...,n, define line L_i: y = (i) x + c_i? But then we need to cover all points. Another idea: Use lines that are all parallel to the hypotenuse? Not allowed. But we could use lines with slopes that are the negatives of each other? Let's think systematically.

The points T are all integer points with a,b ≥ 1 and a+b ≤ n+1. This is essentially a triangular grid. A classic covering is by n lines: all horizontal lines y = 1,...,n. That's all non-sunny. If we want all sunny, we need to avoid horizontal, vertical, slope -1. So we need to find a covering with lines that have slopes not in {0,∞,-1}. This seems plausible if we use lines with slopes that are rational and such that each line passes through multiple points, and we have n lines, so average (n+1)/2 points per line. For odd n, (n+1)/2 is integer. So we can aim for each sunny line to cover exactly (n+1)/2 points. That's a perfect covering. For n=3, (n+1)/2 = 2, and we had lines covering 2 points each. For n=5, (n+1)/2 = 3. So we need 5 sunny lines covering 15 points, each covering 3 points. Is there a set of 5 sunny lines such that each line contains 3 points from T, and the union is all 15 points? That would be a partition of T into 5 subsets each of size 3, each subset collinear on a line. That seems like a combinatorial design. We can attempt to find such lines.

Let's list points for n=5:

a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ 6. The points:

(1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1),(1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). That's 15 points.

We need 5 lines (s sunny) covering all. Possibly we can take lines:

- L1: y = x (slope 1) covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). That's 3 points.
- L2: y = 2x - 1? Let's see: (1,1),(2,3),(3,5) but (3,5) sum 8 >6 not allowed. So only (1,1) and (2,3) maybe (2,3) is allowed? (2,3) sum 5 ≤6, yes. So 2 points. Not enough.
- L2: y = 2x? (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) but (2,4) sum 6 allowed, (3,6) sum 9 >6 not allowed. So (1,2),(2,4) only 2.
- L2: y = (1/2)x + 1/2? That's slope 1/2, covering (1,1),(3,2) but (3,2) sum5 allowed, (5,3) sum8>6 not allowed. That's 2.
- L2: y = -x + something? Not sunny.

We need lines covering 3 points each. The only lines that can cover 3 points are those with slope 1 (y = x + c) if c is such that the points (t, t + c) with t from some range are within T. For c = 0, we get (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) which is 3 points, (4,4) sum8>6 not allowed. So L1 covers 3. For c = -1, y = x - 1 covers (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) but (4,3) sum7>6 not allowed, so 2 points. For c = 1, y = x + 1 covers (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) sum7>6, so 2. So only c=0 yields 3 points. So one line (slope 1, intercept 0) can cover 3 points. Then we need 4 more sunny lines covering the remaining 12 points, each with at most 2 points (since max sunny points for n=5 is 3 but only slope 1 yields 3, other slopes might yield at most 2). So we need 4 lines with at most 2 points each, total capacity 8, but we have 12 points to cover. So impossible? That suggests that for n=5, we cannot have all sunny lines covering all points, because the maximum capacity of sunny lines is limited. But the sum bound (n * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋) for n=5 is 5*3 = 15, which equals N. However that bound is based on the maximum per sunny line (3). But to achieve that bound, we need each sunny line to cover 3 points. However we only have one line (slope 1, intercept 0) that can cover 3 points; other lines cannot achieve 3 points. So the sum bound is not achievable. But is it possible that some sunny lines have slope -1? Not allowed. So indeed, to cover all 15 points with 5 sunny lines, we would need each line to cover exactly 3 points on average. But is it necessary that each line covers 3 points? Not exactly; the union could be covered by lines that cover overlapping points, but total distinct points is 15. If some lines cover fewer than 3 points, then others must cover more than 3 to compensate, but the maximum per line is 3. So the only way to have union size 15 is if each line covers exactly 3 points and those sets are disjoint. However it's also possible that some lines cover 3 points, others cover 2, but then the union might be less than 15 if the lines overlap? Actually if the union is 15, then the sum of capacities (maximum per line) is an upper bound on the union size, but not tight. But we need to find a set of 5 lines such that union size = 15. Since each line can have at most 3 points, the sum of the sizes of the intersections (counting multiplicities) is at most 5*3 = 15, but if some lines have fewer points, then the total sum of sizes (counting multiplicities) is ≤ 15. For union size to be 15, we need that the sum of sizes (counting multiplicities) is at least 15 (since union ≤ sum, but we need union = 15, then sum must be ≥ 15). But the sum of sizes (counting multiplicities) can be > union if overlaps occur. So to have union = 15, we need sum of sizes ≥ 15. Since max sum is 15, the only way to achieve sum ≥ 15 is if each line covers exactly 3 points (sum = 15) and there are no overlaps, i.e., the sets are disjoint. However it's possible that some lines cover 3 points and others cover 2, but then sum < 15, so union ≤ sum < 15, cannot be 15. So indeed for n=5, if all lines are sunny, to cover all 15 points, we need each sunny line to contain exactly 3 points from T, and these 3-point sets must be disjoint. That seems unlikely.

But is it possible that a sunny line contains more than 3 points? For n=5, we argued max is 3. Could a line with slope, say, 3/2 (slope 1.5) contain 3 points? Let's test: y = 1.5x + c, with integer a, need y integer. If slope 3/2, then a must be even to get integer y. For a=2, y = 3 + c; a=4, y = 6 + c. Then a+b ≤ 6. For a=2, b = 3 + c → a+b = 5 + c ≤ 6 → c ≤ 1. For a=4, b = 6 + c → a+b = 10 + c ≤ 6 impossible. So max points is 2. For slope 1/2, similarly. So indeed only slope 1 can give 3 points. So we have one line with 3 points. Then we need to cover 12 points with 4 lines. Each line can cover at most 2 points. So max union size is 3 + 4*2 = 11, which is < 15. Therefore impossible for n=5. But wait, we could use lines with slope 1 but intercepts that give 2 points (like y = x+1 gives (1,2),(2,3) only 2). Could we have a line with slope 2 that covers 3 points? For n=5, max a is 5. For slope 2, y = 2x + c. Points with a = 1,2,3,4,5. b = 2a + c. a+b = 3a + c ≤ 6. For a=1, 3 + c ≤ 6 → c ≤ 3. For a=2, 6 + c ≤ 6 → c ≤ 0. For a=3, 9 + c ≤ 6 impossible. So max points is 2 (a=1 and a=2 if c ≤ 0). So 2.

Thus indeed for n=5, impossible. But for n=3, we had lines with slopes that gave 2 points each, and the union covered all 6 points, with 3 lines. So the maximum sunny points for n=3 is 2, and we had 3 lines, sum capacity 6 = N, and we achieved it with three lines each covering 2 points, disjoint. That worked. So for odd n, is there a construction where each sunny line covers (n+1)/2 points? For n=3, (n+1)/2=2. For n=5, we need each line to cover 3 points, but that seems impossible. So maybe the pattern is: k=n is possible only if n is odd? But we saw n=3 works, n=5 doesn't. So it's not just parity. We need to determine all k for which there exist n lines (with exactly k sunny) covering T. The bound from sum of capacities gives necessary condition for impossibility: if (n - k) * n + k * M < N, then impossible, where M is the maximum points a sunny line can cover. But for k=n, M ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. For n=5, M=3, but the sum bound is N, but the actual achievable maximum union size is less than N due to inability to achieve M for all lines. However we need to consider the possibility that lines can cover overlapping points, but that reduces union size. So to prove impossibility for k=n (when n is even or odd but not covering), we need to show that any set of n sunny lines covers at most something < N. That something is not simply n * M, because it's possible that some sunny lines cover M, others cover less, but we can try to maximize the union. The maximum union size is at most the sum of capacities, but it could be less. But we need an upper bound that is < N. If the sum bound is ≥ N, we cannot conclude impossibility. So we need to find a stronger bound that takes into account the structure of T and the fact that sunny lines cannot be horizontal/vertical/slope -1, which restricts the possible collinearities.

Thus we need to analyze the covering problem more carefully. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to characterize the possible k.

We have n ≥ 3 given. So we can try to construct examples for all k from 0 to n except perhaps some that are impossible. And prove that those impossible k cannot be realized.

Let's attempt to construct for each k. Idea: Use a set of n lines that are all horizontal (or all vertical) which gives k=0. Then we can modify by "replacing" some horizontal lines with sunny lines that still cover the same points (or more). But we need to ensure that the covering condition holds. If we replace a horizontal line with a sunny line that also passes through all the points that were on that horizontal line (or covers them), but we must keep lines distinct. However we can also add extra lines (but we have exactly n lines). So we need to choose n lines. The covering condition is that each point in T is on at least one line. So if we start with n horizontal lines (y=1,...,n), that's a valid covering. Now we want to increase the number of sunny lines. For k > 0, we need to have some sunny lines. We can try to keep some horizontal lines (non-sunny) and add sunny lines that cover points not covered by those horizontal lines. But we need exactly k sunny lines, so the other n-k lines are non-sunny. The covering must be satisfied. So we can try to design a covering where we use, say, (n - k) horizontal lines (or vertical) and then k sunny lines that cover the remaining points, making sure that all points are covered. The challenge is to cover all points with that combination. For large k, we need many sunny lines, but each sunny line covers few points, so we need to have enough non-sunny lines to cover many points. However if we have too many sunny lines, then the number of non-sunny lines is small, and the total capacity of the non-sunny lines might be insufficient to cover all points, but the sunny lines can also cover some points, but we saw for n=5, all sunny lines cannot cover all points. So maybe for n=5, k=5 is impossible, k=4 might be possible? Let's test. For n=5, we need 5 lines with exactly 4 sunny, 1 non-sunny. The non-sunny line could be horizontal (say y=1) covering 5 points. The remaining 10 points need to be covered by 4 sunny lines. Sunny lines can cover at most 3 points each (some may cover 2). So maximum union size from 4 sunny lines is 4*3 = 12, but they could overlap. The total distinct points from non-sunny line is 5, and we need to cover 15 points. The 4 sunny lines could cover at most 12 points, but they may also cover some points that are already covered by the horizontal line, so distinct total ≤ 5 + 12 = 17, but we need to cover 15 points. However we need to check if it's possible to cover all 15 points with 4 sunny lines and 1 horizontal line. The 4 sunny lines can cover at most 12 points, but we have 10 points to cover (since horizontal covers 5). However the 4 sunny lines might also cover some of those 5, but that doesn't help to cover the remaining 10. So we need the 4 sunny lines to cover all points that are not covered by the horizontal line. That's 10 points. So we need 4 sunny lines covering 10 points, each sunny line can cover at most 3 points, so maximum union from 4 sunny lines is at most 12, but we need exactly 10. That's feasible in terms of capacity. But can we actually find 4 sunny lines such that their union (with horizontal line) covers all 15 points? That seems plausible. For n=5, we could try horizontal line y=1 covering (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). Then the remaining points are:

(1,2),(2,2),(1,3),(2,3),(3,2),(1,4),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(1,5),(2,4) already? Let's list all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤6:

(1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1),(1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So after covering y=1 (i.e., b=1), points with b=1 are covered. So remaining points: (1,2),(2,2),(1,3),(2,3),(3,2),(1,4),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(1,5). That's 10 points. We need 4 sunny lines covering these 10 points. We can attempt to design lines that each cover 2 or 3 points. For instance, line L1: y = x (slope 1) covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (1,1) is already covered, (2,2) is in set, (3,3) is in set, so covers 2 points (2,2) and (3,3). That's 2. L2: y = 2x - 1 covers (1,1),(2,3) but (1,1) covered, (2,3) is in set. That's 1. L3: y = -2x + 5 covers (2,1),(1,3) but (2,1) covered, (1,3) is in set. That's 1. L4: y = (1/2)x + 1/2 covers (1,1),(3,2) but (1,1) covered, (3,2) is in set. That's 1. So not enough.

We need 4 lines covering 10 points. The maximum each line can cover is 3, so we need to achieve average 2.5. So some lines must cover 3 points. But which sunny lines can cover 3 points? For n=5, only slope 1 with intercept 0 covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) (3 points). That line also covers (1,1) which is already covered, but we can still count it as covering 2 of the remaining (2,2),(3,3). That's 2 points, not 3. If we take slope 1 with intercept -1, it covers (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) but (2,1) is covered, (3,2) is in set, (4,3) is in set? (4,3) sum 7 >6, not allowed. So only 2 points. Slope 1 with intercept 1 covers (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not allowed, so 2 points. So maximum points from a sunny line within T (without considering coverage of horizontal line) is 3 for slope 1 intercept 0, but that line includes (1,1) which is covered by horizontal line. So the set of remaining points it covers is at most 2. So perhaps we can design lines that are not slope 1 but still cover 3 points? For n=5, is there any line that contains 3 lattice points from T? Let's test systematically: The points are integer points in the triangle. A line containing 3 points must have slope rational with denominator q such that there are at least 3 integer points on the line within the triangle. The possible slopes are those where the difference vectors are multiples of (q,p). For q=1, slope integer, then points are spaced by 1 in x, so they are of form (a, ma + c). The number of such points with a ≤ n is at most n. But we also need a+b ≤ n+1. For integer slope m, a+b = (m+1)a + c ≤ n+1. For m≥2, (m+1) is at least 3, so a ≤ (n+1 - c)/3. For n=5, that gives at most 2 points (if c ≤ something). For m=1, a ≤ (n+1 - c)/2, which can be up to 3 if c=0. So slope 1 yields 3 points (with c=0). For slope -1, not sunny. So indeed only slope 1 (positive) can achieve 3 points. So the maximum number of points on a sunny line in T is at most 3, achieved by line y = x. So we have one line that can cover 3 points, but it also covers (1,1) which is covered by horizontal line. So when we have a non-sunny horizontal line covering b=1, the sunny line y=x can cover 2 of the remaining points. That's okay. So the total union from 1 horizontal line and 4 sunny lines might still be 15, but we need to design the sunny lines to cover the 10 points. Since we have 4 sunny lines, and each can cover at most 3 points, but many of those points are limited. Let's try to find a covering for n=5 with k=4. Perhaps we can use a different non-sunny line, not horizontal, but maybe slope -1 (non-sunny) to cover many points, and then sunny lines covering the rest. Let's explore possibilities.

But the problem asks to determine all k for which such a set exists. So we need to derive a condition on k based on n. The bound from sum of capacities (n - k) * n + k * M is necessary, but not sufficient. However we can also consider that non-sunny lines can be of three types, each covering at most n points. The sum bound is S(k) = (n - k) * n + k * M. If S(k) < N, then impossible. If S(k) ≥ N, it might be possible. We already computed that for k = n, S(n) = n * M. For n even, M ≤ n/2 (since (n+1)/2 floor is n/2) → S(n) ≤ n*(n/2) = n^2/2. N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2. So S(n) < N for even n. So impossible for even n. For odd n, S(n) = n * ((n+1)/2) = n(n+1)/2 = N. So S(n) = N. But as we saw for n=5, S(5) = 15 = N, but it's impossible because the bound is not achievable; the maximum union size from 5 sunny lines is less than N. So we need a better bound for sunny lines that takes into account the structure, not just the maximum per line, but also the fact that lines cannot be all of type that achieve that bound, and there is a combinatorial limitation.

Thus we need to analyze the covering problem thoroughly.

Let's denote T = {(a,b) ∈ ℤ^2, a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. |T| = N. We have n lines L1,…,Ln. Among them, exactly k are sunny. The others are not sunny, meaning they are parallel to x-axis (horizontal), y-axis (vertical), or line x+y=0 (slope -1). So each non-sunny line has slope ∈ {0, ∞, -1}.

Goal: Determine all k (0 ≤ k ≤ n) for which there exists a set of n distinct lines with exactly k sunny such that T ⊆ ⋃ Li.

We can attempt to construct for all k from 0 up to something, and prove impossibility for larger k. But the example for n=5, k=4 is likely possible. Let's try to design a covering with 1 non-sunny line (say vertical or horizontal) and 4 sunny lines. But we need to be systematic.

Alternative approach: Use a covering with lines that are all of the form x = c (vertical) or y = c (horizontal) or x+y = c (slope -1). These are non-sunny. We can choose a mix. For example, for n=5, we could take the 5 vertical lines x=1,…,5. That's 5 lines, all non-sunny, covering all points (since each point (a,b) with a≤5 is on line x=a). That's k=0. To increase k, we can replace some vertical lines with sunny lines. However we must keep lines distinct and cover all points. The covering condition is that each point lies on at least one line. So if we replace a vertical line with a sunny line that also passes through all points that were on that vertical line (or at least those points), then coverage is preserved. But can we find a sunny line that contains all points (a,b) with a = c (vertical) for a given c? That would be a vertical line, which is not sunny. So cannot. So we need to adjust the covering.

Another idea: Use the covering by n lines of type x = i (vertical) for i=1..n. That's non-sunny. Now if we want some sunny lines, we could add extra lines (but we must have exactly n lines). So we need to select n lines, some of which are sunny. So we cannot simply start from all non-sunny and replace; we need to construct a set with the desired k. We can attempt to design a family of lines that includes both non-sunny and sunny lines. For example, we could take (n - k) lines that are all horizontal (or vertical) covering a large portion, and then add k sunny lines that cover the remaining points. But we need to ensure that the total number of lines is n. So we need to cover all points with that combination.

Let's try to construct for n=5, k=4. We need 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line could be, say, the line y = 1 (horizontal) covering points with b=1. That's 5 points. The remaining 10 points (with b ≥ 2) need to be covered by 4 sunny lines. As we saw, the maximum union size from 4 sunny lines is limited. But maybe we can choose a non-sunny line that is not horizontal, but something like x+y = 2 (slope -1) covering (1,1) only. That's not helpful. We need a line that covers many points. The three types each can cover up to n points. The line y = 1 covers 5 points. The line x = 1 covers 5 points. The line x+y = 2 covers 1 point. So best is horizontal or vertical, covering n points. So we can take one horizontal line, say y = 1, covering all points with b=1. That's n points. Then the remaining points (with b ≥ 2) are N - n = n(n+1)/2 - n = n(n-1)/2. For n=5, that's 10. So we need to cover those with k sunny lines. So we need to see whether we can cover the "upper" part of T with k sunny lines. So the problem reduces to: For a given n, determine the maximum number of points from T (with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1) that can be covered by k sunny lines (with the rest of the lines being non-sunny, but we are focusing on the sunny lines covering a subset). But we also have the freedom to choose which non-sunny lines we use; we could use more than one non-sunny line to cover more points, thus reducing the burden on sunny lines. So for a given k, we can allocate some lines to be non-sunny (maybe all of the non-sunny lines) to cover many points, and then the sunny lines cover the rest. So the question is: for which k does there exist a covering where exactly k lines are sunny, and the other n-k lines are non-sunny (any type), such that T is covered. This is a covering problem with constraints on line slopes.

We can try to construct for each possible k, but we need to prove impossibility for some k. The necessary condition from sum of capacities (n - k) * n + k * M is that it must be ≥ N. But as we saw, for k = n (all sunny), this necessary condition is S(n) = n * M, and for odd n, S(n) = N, but it's not sufficient. For even n, it's < N, so impossible. So k = n is impossible for even n, and possible for odd n? We need to test n=3 (odd) works. n=5 (odd) doesn't work according to our reasoning. So maybe it's not all odd n; maybe it's only n=3? That seems unlikely. Let's test n=7 (odd). N = 7*8/2 = 28. M (max sunny points) = floor((n+1)/2) = 4. So n * M = 28 = N. So sum bound is sufficient. But can we cover all points with 7 sunny lines? Probably we need to see if there exists a set of 7 sunny lines that cover all points. For n=7, the points are a,b≥1, a+b≤8. The maximum points on a sunny line is 4 (achieved by slope 1 with intercept 0 covering (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4)). That's 4 points. For other slopes, fewer. So we have one line with 4 points, but then we need 6 more sunny lines covering the remaining 24 points. Each can cover at most 4, but many will be less. The sum bound is 7*4 = 28, but we cannot achieve that because the maximum union from 7 sunny lines is less than 28 due to inability to cover all points with lines of slope 1 only. But is there a configuration where each sunny line covers 4 points? That would require that there are 7 lines each containing 4 points from T, and the sets are disjoint (since total points is 28). That seems unlikely. So we need to analyze the structure of T and collinearity.

Thus we need to find a condition on k. Perhaps the answer is: all k with 0 ≤ k ≤ n, except k = n if n is even, and also except k = n if n is odd? But we have n=3 works, so that's not. So maybe the answer depends on n mod something. Let's try to construct for n=5 with k=4, and for n=7 with k=6? But we need to determine all k.

Let's attempt to systematically analyze.

We have n lines. The covering condition is that all points in T are covered. We can try to use lines that are all of type "horizontal" or "vertical" or "slope -1". For example, if we take n horizontal lines (y = 1,…,n), that's a valid covering. So k=0 works. Similarly, n vertical lines works. Also, we can take n lines that are all of type "x+y = constant" (slope -1). But those lines have slopes -1, which are not sunny. So k=0 works. So k=0 is always achievable. For k>0, we need to include some sunny lines.

Observation: The points T are all integer points in a triangle. The lines with slopes 0, ∞, -1 are the "boundary" lines of the triangle (horizontal, vertical, and the hypotenuse). So they cover many points. If we want to increase the number of sunny lines, we need to "replace" some of these boundary lines with sunny lines, but then we must cover the points that were covered by those boundary lines with the sunny lines. However we can also use a combination where some boundary lines are present, and sunny lines cover some points that are not covered by them. But we need to cover all points. The challenge is to find a construction for each k (maybe all k) that works. Let's try to see if we can construct for n=5, k=4. I'll attempt to design.

We have 5 lines, exactly 4 sunny. So one line is not sunny. I'll try to use that line to be vertical (x=1) covering points (1,b) with b from 1 to 5 (since a=1). That's 5 points. The remaining points are those with a ≥ 2 and a+b ≤ 6. That's N - 5 = 10 points. So we need to cover those with 4 sunny lines. As we saw, the maximum points a sunny line can cover from T is 3, but we need to cover 10 points. With 4 lines, total capacity is 12, so it's possible. However we need to find 4 sunny lines such that their union covers those 10 points. Let's list points with a ≥ 2, a+b ≤ 6. The points:

(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4) but (2,4) sum 6 allowed, (2,5) sum 7 >6 not allowed, (3,1),(3,2),(3,3) (3,4) sum 7 >6 not allowed, (4,1),(4,2) (4,3) sum 7 >6 not allowed, (5,1). Also (1,?) not covered. So points with a≥2: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1). That's 10 points.

Now we need 4 sunny lines covering these 10 points. Is it possible? Let's attempt to design.

We can use lines with slope 1 (y = x) covers (2,2),(3,3) but (2,2) is in set, (3,3) is in set. That's 2 points. Could also cover (1,1) but that's not in our set. So L1: y = x covers (2,2),(3,3). That's 2. L2: y = 2x - 2? (2,2) already, (3,4) not allowed. So not. L2: y = (1/2)x + 1/2 covers (1,1),(3,2) but (3,2) is in set, (1,1) not. That's 1. L3: y = -2x + 6? That passes through (2,2),(3,1) maybe? (2,2) is covered; (3,1) is in set. So L3: y = -2x + 6 covers (2,2) and (3,1). That's 2. L4: y = -x + 5? That's slope -1, not sunny. So not. L4: y = -x + 6? That's slope -1. So not.

We need to cover all 10 points. Let's list points and see possible collinearities:

Points:

(2,4),(3,3) slope -1? Actually (2,4) to (3,3) slope -1. That's not sunny. (2,4) to (4,2) slope -1. So not.

(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) slope -1. So not.

(2,2),(3,3) slope 1. (2,2),(3,1) slope -1. (2,3),(3,2) slope -1. (2,4),(3,3) slope -1.

So many collinearities are with slope -1, not sunny.

Now consider lines with slope 2: (2,1),(3,5) not allowed. (2,2),(3,4) not allowed. So not.

Slope 1/2: (2,2),(4,3) not allowed. (2,1),(4,2) -> points (2,1) and (4,2) slope 1/2? (2-1)/(4-2) = 1/2, yes. That's sunny. That covers (2,1),(4,2). Also (2,2),(4,3) not allowed, but (2,2) is covered by L1. So L2: y = (1/2)x + 1? At x=2, y=2, so line y = (1/2)x + 1 gives (2,2),(4,3) not allowed. So not. Let's compute line through (2,1) and (4,2): slope (2-1)/(4-2)=1/2, equation y = (1/2)x + 1/2? At x=2, y=1.5 not integer. So that line doesn't contain integer points except maybe (2,1) and (4,2) but y is integer at those x, but is the line passing through (2,1) and (4,2) have equation y = (1/2)x + 1/2, which at x=2 gives y=1.5, not integer. So (2,1) and (4,2) are on that line, but the line is not rational with denominator 1? Actually the slope is 1/2, so the line's equation is y = (1/2)x + c. If we plug (2,1): 1 = 1 + c => c = 0. So y = (1/2)x. That passes through (0,0) and (2,1) and (4,2). That's integer points? At x=2, y=1, at x=4, y=2, yes. So y = (1/2)x is a line with slope 1/2, passing through (2,1),(4,2). That's sunny. So L2: y = (1/2)x covers (2,1),(4,2). Also (1,0.5) not integer, but we only care about integer points. So L2 covers two points.

Now (3,1),(5,2) but (5,2) not in set (sum 7 >6). So not.

Maybe we can use lines like y = 2x - 3? (3,3) is on y=2x-3? 2*3-3=3, so (3,3) covered. Also (2,1) is 2*2-3=1, so (2,1) covered. That's two points. That's sunny (slope 2). So L3: y = 2x - 3 covers (2,1),(3,3). That's 2.

Now (2,3),(3,2) slope -1 not sunny. (2,4),(3,3) slope -1. (3,1),(4,2) slope 1? Actually (3,1) to (4,2) slope 1, but (4,2) is in set, and (3,1) is in set. That's slope 1, sunny. So L4: y = x - 2 covers (3,1),(4,2). Also (2,0) not allowed, (5,3) not allowed. So L4 covers 2 points.

Now we have L1: y = x covers (2,2),(3,3). L2: y = (1/2)x covers (2,1),(4,2). L3: y = 2x - 3 covers (2,1),(3,3). L4: y = x - 2 covers (3,1),(4,2). That's four lines. But are they all sunny? L1 slope 1, sunny. L2 slope 1/2, sunny. L3 slope 2, sunny. L4 slope 1, sunny. However we have some overlapping points: (2,2) covered by L1, (2,1) covered by L2 and L3, (3,3) covered by L1 and L3, (4,2) covered by L2 and L4. But we need to cover all ten points.
